Five Arguments Against the Union of the Word with Jesus
Sanusi presents five arguments refuting the Christian doctrine of union.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand five distinct logical arguments that refute the Christian doctrine of the union of the divine Word with Jesus.

Core Idea
The union of divine and human is logically impossible.

This passage presents five crisp arguments that demonstrate the impossibility of the union of the divine Word with Jesus. Each argument follows a logical structure that leads to an absurdity or contradiction, showing that the doctrine cannot hold. As you read, notice how each argument targets a different aspect of the union claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Get a sense of the flow of arguments.
2
Identify the first argument
The first argument: one meaning cannot inhere in two essences. If it did, it would lead to the non-existence of God.
3
Identify the second argument
The second argument: transfer of an attribute is impossible even for accidental attributes, so how for essential ones?
4
Identify the remaining arguments
The third, fourth, and fifth arguments concern particularisation, necessity vs. possibility, and perfection vs. deficiency. Notice how each leads to an impossibility.
5
Reflect on the overall refutation
Consider how these arguments collectively refute the union doctrine by showing logical contradictions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المعنى الواحد لا يقوم بذاتين ، فيكونُ الباقي بعض الإله لا إلهاً ، وعيسى أيضاً قامَ بهِ بعضُ الإله ، فلا يكون إلهاً ، فقد لزم على مذهبهم عدم الإله . وفيه أيضاً : القولُ بانتقالِ المعنى ، وهو محال على الصفاتِ العرضيَّةِ ، فكيف بما هو نفسي عندهم ؟! وأيضاً : فاختصاص الاتحاد بأقنوم الكلمة دون روح القدس الذي هو أقنوم الحياة ، بل ودونَ الجوهر نفسِهِ.. يحتاج إلى مُخصص . وأيضاً : فالاتحاد إنْ كانَ واجباً لزمَ قِدَمُ الناسوتِ ، وإنْ كانَ جائزاً افتقر إلى مُخصص ، ويلزمُ منهُ جواز زوالِهِ ، فتكون ألوهية عيسى جائزةً ، وذلك يفضي إلى مثلِهِ في واجب الوجود ، وهو محال . وأيضاً : الاتحاد إمَّا أنْ يكونَ وصفَ كمال فيجب للذاتِ الأزليَّةِ أزلاً ، وإنْ كانَ صفة ذمّ فقد وصفوه بالنقائص.
One meaning does not inhere in two essences; for then the remainder would be part of a god, not a god, and Jesus also would have some part of god in him, so he would not be a god. Thus their doctrine entails the non-existence of God. And also in it: the claim of transfer of the attribute, which is impossible for accidental attributes, so how about what is essential according to them?! And also: the particularisation of the union to the hypostasis of the Word, excluding the Holy Spirit who is the hypostasis of life, and even excluding the substance itself, requires a particulariser. And also: if the union were necessary, the humanity would be eternal; if it were possible, it would need a particulariser, and the possibility of its cessation would follow, so the divinity of Jesus would be possible, and that leads to something similar in the Necessary Existence, which is impossible. And also: the union is either a description of perfection, so it would be necessary for the eternal essence eternally, or it is a description of deficiency, then they have described Him with imperfections.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage presents a series of arguments refuting the Christian doctrine of the union of the divine Word (الكلمة) with Jesus. The author begins by stating that a single meaning (here the divine Word) cannot inhere in two distinct essences (the divine essence and the human essence of Jesus). If it did, only part of God would be in Jesus, making Jesus not fully God and leaving the rest of God incomplete—thus destroying the concept of God. Second, the idea of transferring a meaning from one essence to another is impossible even for accidental attributes, let alone essential ones like the Word. Third, why is the union specifically with the hypostasis of the Word, not with the Holy Spirit or with the divine substance itself? This requires a particulariser (مخصص), which is not provided. Fourth, if the union is necessary, then Jesus' humanity would be eternal, which is false; if it is contingent, then it requires a particulariser and its cessation would be possible, implying that the divinity of Jesus could cease, which would then imply the possibility of cessation of divinity in the Necessary Existence—an impossibility. Fifth, if the union is a perfection, it should have been present in God eternally; if it is a defect, then they attribute defect to God. Each argument culminates in an impossibility, refuting the Christian claim.

Key Terms
الاتحاد
المعنى
الذات
الأقنوم
الناسوت
واجب الوجود
مخصص
الصفات العرضية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That union can occur between the divine and human without contradiction or division.
Correction: The arguments show that union leads to logical impossibilities, such as the non-existence of God, the impossibility of attribute transfer, the need for an arbitrary particulariser, possible cessation of divinity, and either absence of eternal perfection or attribution of deficiency to God.

Checkpoint
According to the first argument, what would happen if a single meaning (the Word) inhered in two essences?
A) The meaning would be duplicated.
B) The remainder would be part of a god, not a god, leading to the non-existence of God.
C) The two essences would become one.
D) Jesus would become fully divine.

Key Takeaway
The five arguments show that the union doctrine leads to logical impossibilities, thus cannot be true.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Sanusi argues that if union were a perfection, it would have been eternal. Do you ever treat God as if He needs something from creation—like your worship—to be complete? How will you repent from that today?
?
The second argument says transferring an essential attribute is impossible. Are there beliefs you hold that attribute change or transfer to God? Will you examine your creed tonight and root out any such thought?
?
The fifth argument forces a choice: union is either perfection or deficiency. Is there an area in your life where you are comfortable attributing imperfection to God because it suits your desires? What step will you take to correct that this week?

Sanusi's Refutation: Miracles Cannot Prove Union Without Universality
Sanusi refutes the Christian appeal to miracles by invoking proof universality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi uses the logical principle of universal application of proof to refute the Christian argument that Jesus' miracles uniquely demonstrate the union of the divine Word with his humanity.

Core Idea
Miracles as proof are worthless without universality—otherwise beetles could be gods.

Sanusi now engages directly with the Christian claim that Jesus' miracles uniquely demonstrate the union of the Word with his humanity. He does not merely deny the miracles but attacks the logic behind using them as evidence. The following passage shows how he forces the argument into an absurd conclusion using a simple but powerful logical principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice how Sanusi structures his refutation in a logical sequence.
2
Identify the Christian claim and Sanusi's first counter
The Christian argues that Jesus' miracles specify him for union. Sanusi immediately points to Moses performing similar miracles, challenging the uniqueness.
3
Understand the logical principle
Sanusi invokes the rule that a proof (دليل) must apply universally (طرد) not just in one direction (عكس). This means if miracles prove union, then they must do so for all who perform them.
This principle is crucial for kalam methodology.
4
See the absurd conclusion
If any miracle is proof, then even insects with apparent miracles would have to be candidates for union, leading to the absurdity of beetles being gods.
Sanusi ends with a rhetorical flourish to emphasize the repugnance of the doctrine.
5
Reflect on the argument's strength
Consider how this argument refutes the Christian without denying the miracles themselves. The logic undermines the evidentiary value of miracles for unique metaphysical claims.
This is a classic kalam dialectical move.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً : يطالبون بتخصيص ناسوت عيسى بهذا الاتحادِ دونَ غيره ، فإن قالوا : وجه الاختصاص ما ظهر على يدِهِ مِنْ إحياء الموتى ونحوِه .. وَرَدَ عليهم ما ظهر على يد موسى عليه السلامُ مِنْ إحياء العصا ثعباناً ونحوه ، بل ويلزمهم أن يُجوزوا اتحاد الكلمة بكل حادثٍ حتى الخنافس والحشراتِ ؛ لأنَّ قُصارى ما انعدم منها على أصلهم دليل الاتحادِ ، وبإجماع أرباب العقول أنَّ الدليل يلزم طردُهُ لا عكسه ، فلا يلزم إذاً مِنْ عدمِ دليل الاتحادِ في هذه الحوادثِ عدم المدلول اتحاد الكلمة بها ، وما أخسَّ مذهباً يُفضي إلى تجويز تكونَ الخنفساء والجُعَلُ وغيرهما آلهة !
And also: they are required to specify the humanity of Jesus with this union to the exclusion of others. If they say: the specification is due to what appeared at his hands, such as reviving the dead and the like, it is countered by what appeared at the hands of Moses (peace be upon him), such as turning the staff into a serpent and the like. Moreover, they are compelled to allow the union of the Word with every event, even beetles and insects, because the utmost that is absent from them according to their principle is the proof of union. And by the consensus of rational minds, the proof requires its universal application, not its converse. So the absence of the proof of union in these events does not entail the absence of the thing proven—the union of the Word with them. How base is a doctrine that leads to permitting beetles, dung beetles, and others to be gods!
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage contains a dialectical refutation of the Christian claim that the miracles of Jesus (specifically reviving the dead) prove that the divine Word was uniquely united with him. The author first points out that similar miracles were performed by Moses (turning a staff into a serpent), so consistency would require that Moses also be the subject of union. If the Christian tries to avoid this by saying that miracles are the proof, then any being that performs a miracle (even insects, if they could) could be united with the Word, which is absurd. The author invokes the logical principle that a proof (الدليل) must be universally applicable (يلزم طرده) and not just in one direction (لا عكسه). Therefore, the absence of a known proof for an entity does not disprove that union could occur with it. This forces the Christian into an untenable position: either accept union with all miracle-workers, including beetles, or abandon the argument from miracles. The passage concludes with a rhetorical flourish, condemning the doctrine as base.

Key Terms
الاتحاد
الدليل
الطرد
العكس
المعجزة
الاختصاص
الناسوت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That a particular miracle can serve as unique proof for a metaphysical claim without considering universality.
Correction: Sanusi shows that if a miracle is taken as proof of union, it must apply universally to all miracle-workers, leading to absurdities. Therefore, miracles cannot be used as evidence for unique claims without further qualification.

Checkpoint
What logical principle does Sanusi invoke to refute the Christian argument from miracles?
A) The proof must be specific to each case.
B) The proof requires universal application, not its converse.
C) Miracles are only valid for prophets.
D) The absence of proof proves absence of the thing.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi shows that using miracles as evidence for a unique metaphysical union fails because the proof must be universal, leading to absurd consequences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear of a miracle attributed to a person, do you automatically assume divine approval? How does Sanusi's demand for proof universality challenge that assumption in your own heart? Will you now test every claimed sign by this principle?
?
Where in your own thinking do you fall into the converse fallacy—assuming that because you lack evidence for something, it must be false? Name one belief about God or religion you hold without evidence. Are you willing to apply Sanusi's logical rigor to your own faith?
?
Sanusi calls the Christian doctrine 'base' for leading to absurdity. What doctrines or assumptions in your life would look equally absurd if you applied the same universal principle? Will you honestly examine them today?

Refutation of Alternative Interpretations of Union
Sanusi refutes three non-orthodox explanations of union: mixing, light analogy, and imprinting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why physical analogies like mixing, light, and imprinting fail to explain the union of divine and human natures in Christianity.

Core Idea
Physical analogies cannot capture a metaphysical union.

Sanusi continues his critique of the Christian doctrine of union by examining three alternative explanations offered by Christian theologians. Each analogy—mixing, light, and imprinting—attempts to make the union conceivable, but Sanusi shows that they all rely on properties of bodies, which cannot apply to the divine Word. This lesson walks through each refutation and highlights the underlying principle: the Word is a non-physical property of the eternal Essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three analogies
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, noting the three distinct interpretations: mixing, light from sun, and imprinting in wax.
The passage uses a quotation from al-Muqtarih to present the light analogy.
2
Understand the refutation of mixing
Sanusi rejects mixing because it is a property of bodies, and the Word is not a body. All previous objections also apply.
Mixing requires two physical substances, but the Word is a property of the eternal Essence.
3
Understand the refutation of the light analogy
The light analogy fails because sunlight consists of many luminous bodies, whereas the Word is a single unified property. The analogy does not preserve unity.
The refutation emphasizes the multiplicity of light vs. oneness of the Word.
4
Understand the refutation of imprinting
Imprinting is false because the original engraving does not enter the wax; only a likeness appears. Thus, true union is not achieved.
The distinction between the original and its likeness undermines the claimed identity.
5
Conclude with Sanusi's verdict
After refuting all three, Sanusi declares the doctrine unintelligible and dismisses its proponents as the worst and most deficient in understanding.
The conclusion reinforces that no physical analogy can salvage the doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم مَنْ فسَّرَ هذا الاتحاد بالاختلاط والمزج ؛ كاختلاط الخمرِ والماء ونحوهما مِنَ المائعاتِ ، وجميع ما وَرَدَ على الأَوَّلِ يرد على هذا ، ويزيدُ بأنَّ الاختلاط مِنْ أحكام الأجسامِ ، فكيفَ يُعقَلُ في الكلمة التي هي خاصيَّةُ الذاتِ الأزليَّةِ ؟! قال المقترحُ : ( وسمعتُ مِنْ بعضهم عند المباحثة يقول : نسبتُهُ نسبةُ ضياء الشمسِ مِنَ الشمس ، فهي مشرقة علينا ولم تفارق الشمس ، ولم يعلموا أنَّ أضواء الشمس أجسام مضيئة كثيرة بعضُها يتَصلُ بما أشرقَ عليهِ ، وبعضُها يتّصلُ بغيرِهِ ، وأين هذا مِنَ الخاصّيَّةِ المتحدة ؟! ) ومنهم مَنْ فَسَّرَهُ بالانطباع ؛ كانطباع صورةِ النقشِ في الشمع ، وهذا باطل ؛ فإنَّ نفسَ النقشِ لم يحصل فيما طُبعَ فيه ، وإِنَّما حصل فيه مثاله . فتبيَّنَ أنَّ المذهب غير معقول ، وهم أخس الفرق وأرذلها أفهاماً.
Among them are those who interpreted this union as mixing and blending, like the mixing of wine and water and similar liquids. All that was brought against the first (view) applies to this, and furthermore that mixing is one of the properties of bodies, so how can it be conceived in the Word which is a property of the eternal Essence?! Al-Muqtarih said: 'I heard from some of them during debate saying: its relation is like the relation of sunlight to the sun; it shines upon us and does not separate from the sun. They did not know that the lights of the sun are many luminous bodies, some of which are connected to what they illuminate, and others are connected to something else. Where is this from the unified property?!' And among them are those who interpreted it as imprinting, like the imprinting of the image of an engraving in wax. This is false, for the engraving itself does not occur in what it is imprinted in; rather, its likeness occurs in it. So it has become clear that the doctrine is unintelligible, and they are the worst of sects and the most deficient in understanding.
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage refutes three alternative Christian interpretations of the union. The first interpretation is mixing (الاختلاط) like liquids. The author points out that all the previous objections apply, and adds that mixing is a property of bodies, so it cannot be applied to the divine Word, which is a non-bodily property of the eternal Essence. The second interpretation, quoted from al-Muqtarih, is the analogy of sunlight emanating from the sun without separation. The refutation notes that sunlight consists of many luminous bodies (أضواء الشمس أجسام مضيئة), some connected to what they illuminate, others not—so the analogy fails because the Word is supposed to be a single, unified property (الخاصية المتحدة), not multiple bodies. The third interpretation is imprinting (الانطباع) like an image in wax. This is false because the original engraving does not enter the wax; only its likeness does. Thus, none of these interpretations preserve the claimed union. The passage concludes that the doctrine is unintelligible and its proponents are the worst and most deficient in understanding.

Key Terms
الاختلاط
الانطباع
الضياء
الخاصية
الجسم
المثال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Physical analogies like mixing, light, or imprinting can adequately explain the union of divine and human natures.
Correction: These analogies fail because they rely on properties of bodies, whereas the divine Word is a non-physical property of the eternal Essence. They do not preserve the oneness required for union.

Checkpoint
Why does Sanusi reject the interpretation of union as mixing (اختلاط)?
A) Mixing requires two bodies, but the Word is not a body.
B) Mixing is impossible for liquids.
C) The Word cannot be mixed with Jesus because Jesus is divine.
D) Mixing would make the Word divisible.

Key Takeaway
Physical analogies cannot salvage the doctrine of union because the divine Word is a non-bodily, unified property.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you try to understand God's nature, do you rely on familiar analogies from the physical world? How might you be tempted to reduce the divine to something manageable, and what would it cost you to instead submit to the limits of human comprehension?
?
Sanusi calls those who hold the union doctrine 'the worst of sects and the most deficient in understanding.' Do you possess the intellectual humility to abandon a comfortable belief when it is shown to be incoherent? What cherished analogy about God are you holding onto that needs to be dismantled?
?
The light analogy seemed plausible to its proponents—what plausible but false ideas about God have you absorbed from culture or upbringing? Will you commit today to testing every belief about God against revelation and reason, even if it means letting go of something you love?

Refutation of the Light Analogy for Union
The author rejects the analogy that the Word relates to Christ as sunlight to the sun.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the light analogy fails to prove union and why it actually undermines the claim.

Core Idea
Light spreads as multiple bodies, not a single union.

In theological debates, analogies are often used to explain mysterious doctrines. Proponents of union with Christ once compared the Word's presence to sunlight emanating from the sun. The author here shows why this analogy is flawed, revealing a misunderstanding of the nature of light.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and analogy
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp the analogy: the Word's relation to Christ is like sunlight from the sun, shining without separation.
Notice that the questioner presents the analogy as something he heard from proponents of union.
2
Understand the refutation
The author points out that the lights of the sun are many luminous bodies, not a single characteristic. Some connect to one object, others to different objects.
This refutes the idea of a single, undivided union.
3
See why the analogy fails
Ask yourself: If sunlight is multiple bodies, then the analogy actually supports multiplicity, not union. This undermines the proponents' claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال المقترحُ : ( وسمعتُ مِنْ بعضهم عند المباحثة يقول : نسبتُهُ نسبةُ ضياء الشمسِ مِنَ الشمس ، فهي مشرقة علينا ولم تفارق الشمس ، ولم يعلموا أنَّ أضواء الشمس أجسام مضيئة كثيرة بعضُها يتَصلُ بما أشرقَ عليهِ ، وبعضُها يتّصلُ بغيرِهِ ، وأين هذا مِنَ الخاصّيَّةِ المتحدة ؟! )

And they do not know that the lights of the sun are many luminous bodies, some of them connect to what they illuminate, and some connect to other things. And where is this from the single characteristic?!'
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from a questioner who recounts an analogy used by proponents of union: the divine Word's relation to Christ is like sunlight emanating from the sun—it shines on us without parting from the sun. The author then refutes this by pointing out that the lights of the sun are in fact many luminous bodies, not a single indivisible characteristic. Some of these lights connect to the object illuminated, others to different objects. Thus, the analogy fails to demonstrate a single, undivided union.

Misconception & Correction

?

Refutation of Union as Impression (انطباع)
The author rejects the analogy of a seal's impression, noting only a likeness is transferred.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the analogy of a seal's impression fails to support true union and how it actually undermines the claim.

Core Idea
An impression only transfers a copy, not the original.

Some argued that the union of the divine Word with Christ is like an impression left by a seal in wax. In this passage, the author refutes this by pointing out that in an impression, the original engraving does not enter the wax; only a likeness appears. Thus, the analogy would imply that the Word is not truly present but merely represented.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the argument.
Notice the key terms: انطباع, نقش, شمع, مثال.
2
Identify the refuted view
Some interpret union as impression (انطباع). Understand what this means.
3
Understand the analogy
The seal's engraving impresses an image in wax. Recognize the analogy.
4
See the flaw
The author says the actual engraving does not transfer; only a likeness (مثال) occurs. This is the key refutation.
5
Apply to union
If union were impression, the divine Word would not be truly united but only represented. Thus the interpretation fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم مَنْ فَسَّرَهُ بالانطباع ؛ كانطباع صورةِ النقشِ في الشمع ، وهذا باطل ؛ فإنَّ نفسَ النقشِ لم يحصل فيما طُبعَ فيه ، وإِنَّما حصل فيه مثاله.
And among them are those who interpreted it as impression, like the impression of the image of an engraving in wax. And this is false; for the actual engraving itself does not occur in that on which it is impressed; rather, its likeness occurs in it.
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage refutes the interpretation of union as impression (intiba'), using the example of a seal stamping an image into wax. The author argues that when a seal impresses an image, the actual engraving itself (the original) does not transfer into the wax; only a likeness or image of it appears. Similarly, if union were an impression, the divine Word itself would not actually be present in Christ, but only a likeness. This undermines the claim of a true union.

Key Terms
انطباع
نقش
شمع
مثال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Impression transfers the original object into the impressed material.
Correction: The author explains that only a likeness is transferred, not the original engraving.

Checkpoint
What is the key problem with the impression interpretation of union?
A) Wax cannot receive impressions.
B) The impression is not the original, only a likeness.
C) The seal must remain attached.
D) Engraving is not a good analogy.

Key Takeaway
The impression analogy undermines true union because it only conveys a likeness, not the original.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you have ever thought of God's relationship to creation as a kind of stamp leaving an impression on the world, how does this passage challenge that assumption? Are you willing to let go of any mental image that reduces God to a physical process?
?
When you think of the Word of God coming to you (in the Qur'an), do you sometimes feel that only a 'copy' of the true Word has reached you? How does this lesson's insistence that the original does not transfer impact your confidence in the revelation—or your sense of distance from God?
?
This passage denies that mere likeness suffices for real presence. In your worship, do you sometimes settle for a 'likeness' of devotion—routine gestures without the original sincerity? What would it mean for you to seek the real presence of God in your prayer, not a mere impression?

Refutation of Christian Crucifixion Doctrine
Refutes crucifixion doctrine using logic and Gospel.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how logical reasoning and Gospel texts refute the Christian claim of Jesus' divinity and crucifixion.

Core Idea
Jesus' own words in the Gospel prove he is a servant, not God.

This passage concludes the refutation of the Christian doctrine of union (hulul) by exposing its logical absurdities and contradictions. The author presents a dilemma: if Jesus is God, how can God be crucified? Christians respond by distinguishing between human and divine natures, but this raises further problems. Then, the author cites the Gospel itself to show that Jesus worshipped God, affirming that he is not divine.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dilemma
The author challenges Christians: if Jesus is God, how can he be crucified? Notice the logical contradiction.
2
Examine the Christian response
Christians say only the human nature was crucified. Ask: if the natures are united, can one suffer without the other?
3
Analyze the Gospel quote
Jesus says 'my God and your God.' This shows he has a God, so he cannot be God. Note the author's interpretation of 'Father'.
The author explains 'Father' means provider, not literal paternity.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes that the doctrine is unintelligible and Christians are among the worst in understanding.
This is a strong rhetorical verdict.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فتبيَّنَ أنَّ المذهب غير معقول ، وهم أخس الفرق وأرذلها أفهاماً ، وإدراك الحقائقِ على مثلهم عسير ، وقد قالوا : إِنَّ صُلب عيسى فقيل لهم : كيف يُصلَبُ الإلهُ ؟ فقالوا : المصلوب الناسوت ، فقيل لهم : كيفَ ينفرد بالصَّلْبِ الناسوتُ دونَ اللاهوتِ وقد اتحدا ؟! ثم قد ورد في إنجيلهم ما يشير إلى تعبد المسيح وخضوعه وخشوعِهِ للرب سبحانَهُ ، والتزامِهِ أحكام العبيدِ ؛ مِنَ التَذلُّلِ وطلب الجزاء مِنَ اللهِ تعالى ، حتى قال : أنا ماض إلى أبي وأبيكم ، وإلهي وام[؟] ، فإن كانوا يتمسكون بلفظ ( أبي ) فقد قال : ( وأبيكم ) ، فبالمعنى الذي أثبت الأبوَّةَ لهم مِنَ التربية واللطفِ يثبتُ له ، وقوله : ( وإلهي ) تصريح بإثباتِ الألوهية لغيرِهِ.
Thus it became clear that the doctrine is unintelligible, and they are the meanest of sects and the worst in understanding. The perception of truths among them is difficult. And they said that 'Isa (Jesus) was crucified. So it was said to them: How can a god be crucified? They said: The one crucified is the human nature (nasut). So it was said to them: How can the human nature alone be crucified without the divine nature (lahut) when they are united?! Moreover, it is stated in their Gospel that indicates the worship, submission, and humility of Christ to the Lord, and his adherence to the rules of servants, such as humiliation and seeking reward from Allah, until he said: I am going to my Father and your Father, and my God and your[?] God. If they cling to the word 'my Father,' he also said 'your Father.' So by the meaning in which he affirmed fatherhood for them of upbringing and kindness, it is affirmed for him. And his saying 'my God' is a clear affirmation of divinity for other than Him.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage concludes the refutation of Christian union doctrine by highlighting its absurdities and contradictions. The author first states that the doctrine is unintelligible. He then points out the logical dilemma regarding the crucifixion: if Jesus is God, how can God be crucified? Christians respond that only the human nature (nasut) was crucified, but the author counters that if the two natures are united, it is impossible for one to suffer without the other. Furthermore, the author cites the Gospel where Jesus says he is going to 'my Father and your Father, and my God and your God.' This proves that Jesus worships a God distinct from himself. The author explains that 'Father' here means provider and caretaker, not literal paternity. Thus, Jesus affirms divinity for another, not himself.

لاهوت
إنجيل
أبوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Christians believe that Jesus calling God 'my Father' proves he is divine.
Correction: The author points out that Jesus also says 'your Father' to his disciples, so the term is used figuratively for upbringing, and more importantly, Jesus says 'my God,' which shows he worships God, so he cannot be God.

Checkpoint
How does the author use the Gospel quote to refute the divinity of Jesus?
A) Jesus says he will return to his Father alone.
B) Jesus says 'my God and your God,' showing he has a God above him.
C) Jesus denies being crucified.
D) Jesus calls all believers gods.

Key Takeaway
Jesus' own words in the Gospel prove he is a servant of God, not God himself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The author calls the Christian doctrine 'unintelligible' and its followers 'the meanest in understanding'. But have you ever held onto a belief that was logically inconsistent simply because it was familiar? What in your own life might you be clinging to that contradicts clear evidence from the Qur'an or sound reasoning?

Understanding Sufi Annihilation and Ecstatic Utterances
The author explains the state of annihilation (fanā') and excuses ecstatic utterances.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of spiritual annihilation (fanā') and why some scholars excuse ecstatic utterances like 'I am the Truth' as products of spiritual intoxication rather than doctrinal claims.

Core Idea
Ecstatic utterances are excused as involuntary outbursts during spiritual annihilation, not literal theology.

This passage addresses a sensitive issue in Sufism: seemingly blasphemous utterances by mystics. The author explains that such words arise during a state of fanā', where the seeker is overwhelmed by divine presence and loses self-awareness. Understanding this state is crucial for proper evaluation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed utterances
Read the Arabic passage and note the phrases 'ما في الجبة إلا الله' and 'أنا الحق'. These are the ecstatic utterances (shatahāt) attributed to some Sufis.
2
Understand the state of fanā'
Learn the definition of fanā': a state where the seeker witnesses only God, self becomes absent. This is a state of sukr (intoxication).
3
See the excuse and return to sobriety
Note that when the seeker returns to sahw (sobriety), such utterances cease. Scholars of the path interpret these figuratively and excuse them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عبارات بعض الصوفية التي ظاهرُها الاتحاد قالوها في حالة فناء ] وعزا بعض أصحاب المقالاتِ إلى بعض الصوفية القول بالاتحادِ وربَّما أخذوا ذلكَ مِنْ شطحاتٍ تُنقَلُ عن بعضهم؛ كقولهم: ( ما في الجُبَّةِ إلا الله )، و ( أنا الحقُّ ) ونحو ذلك، وبعضُ علماء الطريقِ يتأوَّلُ لهم ويُنزّهُهم عنِ القولِ بمثل هذه المقالة، ويقولُ: إِنَّ السالك ربَّما طرأت عليهِ حالةٌ لا يُشاهِدُ فيها غيرَ اللهِ تعالى، فتغيبُ نفسُهُ عنهُ فضلاً عن غيرها، ويُعبّرون عن هذه الحالة بالفناء، فيجري على لسانه مثلُ هذهِ الألفاظ، وهي حال سُكْرٍ وغلبة، وإذا رجع إلى صحوه وإحساس نفسه.. لم يصدر منه شيءٌ مِنْ ذلكَ، ويُعذَر بذلك (1) ومنهم مَنْ واخذهم بذلك، وحكم بالقتل؛ كفتوى الجنيد في الحلاج (2)

(1) انظر ( المقصد الأسنى ) للإمام الغزالي ( ص ٢٤٩ ، ٣٠٨ )
(2) تبع المصنف هنا العلامة ابن التلمساني في شرح معالم أصول الدين » ( ص ۲۳۹ ) ، ولكن الجنيد مات سنة ( ۲۹۸ هـ ) ، وقتل الحلاج سنة ( ٣٠٩هـ ) ، قال العلامة الحافظ المؤرخ اليافعي في «مرآة الجنان » ( ٢/ ١٩٤ ) : ( ما قيل : إن الجنيد وابن داود الظاهري من جملة من أفتى بقتله .. لا يصح ؛ لأن الجنيد توفي سنة ثمان وتسعين ومئتين ، قبل قتل الحلاج بإحدى عشرة سنة ، ومحمد بن داود توفي قبل قصة الحلاج باثنتي عشرة سنة ) .
[Expressions of some Sufis whose apparent meaning is union (ittihad), said in a state of annihilation (fanā')] And some authors of doctrinal works attributed to some Sufis the belief in union (ittihād), and perhaps they took that from ecstatic utterances (shatahāt) transmitted from some of them, such as their saying: 'There is nothing in the cloak but God,' and 'I am the Truth (anā al-ḥaqq),' and the like. Some scholars of the path (Ṭarīqa) interpret (ta'wīl) for them and exonerate them from saying such a statement, and say: The seeker (sālik) may sometimes experience a state in which he witnesses nothing but God Almighty, so his self becomes absent from him, let alone others; they call this state annihilation (fanā'). Then such words flow upon his tongue, and it is a state of intoxication (sukr) and overwhelming. When he returns to his sobriety (ṣaḥw) and awareness of himself, nothing of that issues from him, and he is excused for it (1). And among them are those who hold them accountable for it and judge them deserving of death, such as the fatwa of al-Junayd regarding al-Ḥallāj (2).

(1) See: al-Maqṣad al-Asnā by Imām al-Ghazālī, pp. 249, 308.
(2) The author here follows the scholar Ibn al-Tilimsānī in his commentary on Maʿālim Uṣūl al-Dīn (p. 239), but al-Junayd died in 298 AH, and al-Ḥallāj was killed in 309 AH. The scholar, historian, and memorialist al-Yāfiʿī said in Mir’āt al-Jinān (2/194): 'What is said that al-Junayd and Ibn Dāwūd al-Ẓāhirī were among those who issued a fatwa for his killing... is not correct, because al-Junayd died in 298 AH, eleven years before al-Ḥallāj's execution, and Muḥammad ibn Dāwūd died twelve years before the affair of al-Ḥallāj.'
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage discusses accusations of belief in union (ittihād) against some Sufis, based on their ecstatic utterances (shatahāt) such as 'I am the Truth' or 'There is nothing in the cloak but God.' The author explains that some scholars of the spiritual path interpret these utterances figuratively, attributing them to a state of spiritual annihilation (fanā') where the seeker is overwhelmed by the presence of God and speaks without control. This is a state of intoxication (sukr), and when the person returns to sobriety (ṣaḥw), they do not repeat such claims. Therefore, they are excused. However, some scholars, like al-Junayd, are reported to have held such speakers accountable and even issued fatwas of death, as in the case of al-Ḥallāj. The author includes a footnote questioning the historical accuracy of that fatwa, noting that al-Junayd died years before al-Ḥallāj's execution.

Key Terms
الصوفية
الشطحات
الفناء
السكر
الصحو

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sufis who utter 'I am the Truth' literally believe they are God.
Correction: The author explains these utterances are said in a state of spiritual intoxication and are not doctrinal statements.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why do some Sufis utter phrases like 'I am the Truth'?
A) They are in a state of spiritual annihilation (fanā')
B) They deliberately profess union with God
C) They are mentally ill
D) They are quoting scripture

Key Takeaway
Ecstatic utterances by Sufis are interpreted as involuntary expressions during spiritual annihilation and are excused.

The Debate on Accountability: Al-Junayd and Al-Ḥallāj
The author presents the opposing view of accountability and casts doubt on the al-Junayd fatwa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opposing view that holds Sufis accountable for ecstatic utterances, as illustrated by the fatwa of al-Junayd regarding al-Ḥallāj, and the historical question about its authenticity.

Core Idea
Not all scholars excuse ecstatic utterances; some demand accountability, but historical details are contested.

While some scholars excuse ecstatic utterances as products of spiritual intoxication, others hold the speaker fully accountable. This lesson examines the reported fatwa of al-Junayd against al-Ḥallāj and the historical debate about its accuracy, as noted by the author.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opposing view
Read the part of the passage that mentions those who hold the Sufis accountable and judge them deserving of death. Note the example of al-Junayd and al-Ḥallāj.
2
Examine the historical footnote
The author includes a footnote that questions the authenticity of al-Junayd's fatwa, citing historical dates. Understand why this matters for evaluating the reliability of the report.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider how the two approaches (excusing vs. condemning) reflect different attitudes toward mystical experience and the role of the law.

Erroneous Views of Hashwiyya and Karramiyya on God's Direction
Sanusi reports erroneous positions of Hashwiyya and Karramiyya on God's direction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the erroneous positions of the Hashwiyya and Karramiyya regarding God's spatial relation to the Throne and why they violate divine transcendence.

Core Idea
Some sects imagined God sitting on the Throne like a body—this lesson exposes their mistakes.

In this passage, Sanusi reports the views of two early sectarian groups that fell into anthropomorphism. The Hashwiyya took the direction 'above' literally, and the Karramiyya debated whether God is in physical contact with the Throne or separate from it. Understanding these errors helps clarify the orthodox position that Allah is not confined to space.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall report.
Notice the sect names and their differing opinions.
2
Identify the Hashwiyya's position
The Hashwiyya specified a direction (above) and interpreted 'istiwā'' literally. Note how this leads to corporealism.
3
Analyze the Karramiyya subdivisions
The Karramiyya disagreed among themselves: some said God is in contact with the Throne, others said separate. Among those who said separate, some said finite distance, others infinite.
This shows confusion even within the sect.
4
Reflect on the common error
Ask yourself why these views are rejected. The commentary explains that they compromise divine transcendence by implying spatial limitations.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحشوية، وعيّنوا من الجهات جهة فوق. ثم اختلفت الكرامية بعد ذلك؛ فمنهم من قال: إنه مماس للعرش ذلك تعالى [؟] عن ومنهم من زعم أنه مباين له، ثم اختلف هؤلاء؛ فمنهم من زعم أنه مباين بمسافة متناهية، ومنهم من زعم أنه مباين بمسافة غير متناهية. والحشوية حملت الاستواء في الآية على ظاهره، وامتنعت من التأويل، وسيأتي إن شاء الله تعالى الكلام على بعض ما أشكل من ظاهر القرآن والحديث في موضع أليق به من هذا.
And the Hashwiyya specified a direction from among the directions as 'above'. Then the Karramiyya differed after that: among them are those who said that He is in contact with the Throne — exalted is He above that — and among them are those who claimed that He is separate from it. Then these differed: among them are those who claimed that He is separate by a finite distance, and among them are those who claimed that He is separate by an infinite distance. And the Hashwiyya interpreted the 'istiwā'' in the verse [literally] according to its apparent meaning, and refrained from ta'wīl (interpretation). And there will come, God willing, discussion of some of what is problematic from the apparent [meaning] of the Qur'an and hadith in a place more appropriate for it than this.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage is a doxographical report describing the positions of two deviant sects: the Hashwiyya and the Karramiyya. It begins by stating that the Hashwiyya assigned a specific direction (jiha) to God, namely 'above' (fawq). Then it explains that the Karramiyya, after that, disagreed among themselves: some said God is in physical contact (mumāss) with the Throne, while others said He is separate (mubāyin) from it. Among those who said separate, some said the separation is by a finite distance, others by an infinite distance. The passage then returns to the Hashwiyya, noting that they took the word 'istiwā'' in the Qur'anic verse (about God's establishment on the Throne) literally according to its outward meaning and refused any figurative interpretation (ta'wīl). The author concludes by promising a later discussion of problematic apparent meanings from the Qur'an and hadith in a more suitable place. The passage thus catalogues anthropomorphic and corporealist tendencies that compromise divine transcendence.

Key Terms
الحشوية
الكرامية
الجهة
الاستواء
المماسة
المباينة
التأويل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Assigning a direction (like 'above') to God is an acceptable literal interpretation of certain Qur'anic verses.
Correction: The passage shows that such literalism leads to corporealism and is rejected by mainstream Sunni theology because Allah is not confined to space or direction.

Checkpoint
What did the Karramiyya disagree about regarding God and the Throne?
A) Whether God is eternal or originated
B) Whether God is in contact with the Throne or separate, and if separate, by finite or infinite distance
C) Whether the Throne is created or uncreated
D) Whether God's attributes are identical to His essence

Key Takeaway
The Hashwiyya and Karramiyya fell into error by attributing spatial and physical properties to Allah, a violation of tanzih (divine transcendence).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
When you recite verses like 'Ar-Rahman 'alal 'arsh istawa', does any part of you feel uneasy not picturing a physical sitting? This lesson shows the Hashwiyya refused ta'wil—are you willing to submit to the understanding of Ahl al-Sunna even if it feels uncomfortable?
?
If a family member or friend firmly believes that Allah is sitting on the Throne like a king on a throne, would you have the courage to gently explain the danger of that belief using this passage? What exactly would you say to them, and would your fear of conflict stop you?

Mu'tazilite Denial of Seeing God and Promise of Refutation
Sanusi reports the Mu'tazilite view that God is not seen and their reasoning, promising a later refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite reasoning for denying the vision of God (ru'ya) and their promise of a later refutation in the text.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilites deny seeing God because vision requires physical conditions that cannot apply to Him.

In this passage, Sanusi presents the position of a group of Mu'tazilites who argue that God cannot be seen. They base this on a theory of vision that requires physical rays, a specific anatomical structure, and facing. The author notes that later, in the chapter on the merit of vision, he will refute this view. This lesson introduces the opposing doctrine before its rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the phrase 'la yura, kama annahu la yara' which expresses their denial both ways.
2
Identify the conditions for vision
The Mu'tazilites require three conditions: connection of rays (ittisal al-ash'ah), emission from a specific structure (inbi'ath min bunyah makhsusah), and facing (muqabalah) or its equivalent. Understand why these make vision of God impossible for them.
3
Note the promise of refutation
The author says he will refute this view later in the section on the merit of vision (fadl al-ru'ya). This sets up the structure of the text and the author's intention to defend the Sunni position.
The refutation will come later; this is just the statement of the opposing view.
4
Reflect on the common misunderstanding
Some think the Mu'tazilites only deny that God can be seen, but the text shows they also deny that God sees (la yara). This is a key nuance to remember.
The passage says 'is not seen, just as He does not see' - both are denied.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصارَ بعضُ المعتزلة إلى أنَّ البارى جلَّ وعلا عما يقولُ الظالمونَ علوّاً كبيراً لا يرى ، كما أنَّهُ لا يُرى ، وهو قياس مذهبهم في اشتراط اتصال الأشعة ، وانبعاثها مِنْ بِنْيَةٍ مخصوصة ، والمقابلة أو ما في حكمها في الرؤية ، وسيأتي إنْ شاءَ الله تعالى في فضل الرؤية إبطال مذهبهم في ذلك بأشبع قول
And some of the Mu'tazila held that the Creator, exalted and high above what the wrongdoers say, is not seen, just as He does not see, and it is the analogical extension of their school upon the condition of the connection of rays, and their emission from a specific structure, and facing or what is in its ruling concerning vision. And it will come, God willing, in the chapter on the merit of vision, the refutation of their school in that with a most satisfying statement.
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage reports the Mu'tazilite position on the vision of God (ru'ya). The author states that some Mu'tazilites held that God is not seen (lā yurā), just as He does not see (lā yarā). Their reasoning is based on their school's conditions for vision: the connection of rays emitted from a specialized physical structure (like an eye), and the necessity of facing (muqābala) or its equivalent. Since these conditions cannot apply to God, they conclude that vision of Him is impossible. The author then promises that later, in the section on the merit of vision, he will refute this view with a thorough argument. This passage thus serves as a statement of the opposing view before its rebuttal.

Correction: The text shows they hold that God is not seen just as He does not see (lā yarā), affirming that they deny both.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite denial of the vision of God rests on a physical theory of vision that cannot apply to the transcendent Creator.

Mu'tazila on Divine Attributes and Speech
Mu'tazila deny essential attributes except speech, which they reinterpret as created.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila position on divine attributes, especially their view that God's speech is created rather than eternal.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila affirm essential attributes but deny that God's speech is eternal—they make it created.

The Mu'tazila, a major theological school, agreed with Ahl al-Sunna that God has attributes, but they differed on how those attributes inhere. Their most distinctive departure was on divine speech, which they insisted must be created. This passage from the coursebook lays out their reasoning and points out the source of their error.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage has two clear parts: first about attributes in general, then about speech.
2
Identify the two-part position
The passage presents the Mu'tazila view in two parts: (1) their stance on essential attributes (they are necessary by essence, not by a meaning), and (2) their exception for speech, which they reinterpret as created letters and sounds.
3
Understand the reasoning for created speech
The Mu'tazila argue that speech cannot subsist in God because it would then be originated (hadith), and originated things cannot subsist in the Eternal. So they say God is a speaker by creating speech in another locus.
The author traces their error to restricting speech to letters and sounds.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثبوت الأحوالِ عند المعتزلة لا لمعنى ملازم لها قالوا : وهذه الأوصافُ واجبةٌ لهُ تعالى لذاته ، لا لمعنى ملازم لها يقوم بذاتِهِ جلَّ وعلا كما هو في حقنا ، واستثنوا مِنْ ذلكَ كونَهُ تعالى مُتكلّماً ، فوافقوا على أنه تعالى متكلم بكلام ، لكن خالفوا أهل السنَّةِ في معنى الكلام الثابت له تعالى ، فهم جعلوه حروفاً وأصواتاً يخلقها جلَّ وعلا في محل مِنَ الأجرام يتكلم بها ، ولا يقوم به هذا الكلام عندهم ؛ لأنَّه لا يكون إلا حادثاً ، وقيام الحوادث بذاتِهِ محال ؛ فمعنى كونِهِ مُتكلّماً عندَهم : أنَّه خالق للكلام في غيرِه ، وجاءَهم هذا الفسادُ مِنْ حصرِهم الكلام في الحروفِ والأصوات وسيأتي تحقيق القولِ معهم في ذلكَ إِنْ شاءَ الله تعالى
The affirmation of states (al-ahwal) according to the Mu'tazila is not by a meaning (ma'na) subsisting in them. They said: These attributes are necessary for Him, exalted, by His essence, not by a meaning subsisting in His essence, glorified and exalted, as is the case with us. And they excepted from that His being, exalted, a speaker (mutakallim). So they agreed that He, exalted, is a speaker with speech, but they disagreed with Ahl al-Sunna regarding the meaning of speech established for Him, exalted. For they made it letters and sounds which He, exalted, creates in a locus among bodies by which He speaks, and this speech does not subsist in Him according to them because it cannot be but originated (hadith), and the subsistence of originated things in His essence is impossible. So the meaning of His being a speaker according to them is that He is the creator of speech in other than Him. And this corruption came to them from their restricting speech to letters and sounds, and the verification of the statement with them concerning that will come, if Allah wills.
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage defines the Mu'tazila position on divine attributes and their specific view on God's speech. It begins by stating the position of the Mu'tazila (also called Ahl al-I'tizal) regarding the 'states' (ahwal). They affirm certain attributes (like being knowing, powerful) as necessary for God by His essence, not because of a meaning (ma'na) subsisting in Him—unlike Ash'aris who posit such meanings. However, they except the attribute of speech (kalam): they agree that God is a speaker (mutakallim), but disagree with Ahl al-Sunna on what that speech is. According to the Mu'tazila, God's speech consists of letters and sounds that He creates in some physical locus (e.g., a body, like the burning bush) and He speaks by means of that created speech. This speech does not subsist in God's essence because it would then be originated (hadith) and origination in the essence of the Eternal is impossible. Thus they redefine being a speaker as creating speech elsewhere. The author notes that this error arises from restricting speech to letters and sounds—a point that will be fully refuted later.

Key Terms
الأحوال (al-ahwal)
المعتزلة (al-Mu'tazila)
الكلام (al-kalam)
المتكلّم (al-mutakallim)
الحروف والأصوات (al-huruf wa al-aswat)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila completely deny that God speaks.
Correction: They affirm that God is a speaker (mutakallim), but they define it as His creating speech in another locus, not as an eternal attribute.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila, how is God said to be a speaker (mutakallim)?
A) By an eternal speech subsisting in His essence
B) By being the creator of speech in another locus
C) By being the speaker of an eternal attribute
D) By being identical with speech

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila affirm essential attributes of God by essence but deny an eternal speech attribute, claiming God's speech is created letters and sounds in a separate locus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Quran, do you take it as the eternal speech of Allah directly from His essence, or as a created message? What impact would it have on your reverence for the Quran if you believed it was created at some point in time?
?
The Mu'tazila concluded that God cannot be the locus of originated things. Have you ever placed human-defined limits on what Allah can or cannot do, based on your own reasoning? Where in your life do you need to submit more fully to Allah's absolute power?
?
The passage identifies the root error of the Mu'tazila as restricting speech to letters and sounds. In what ways might you be restricting how Allah speaks to you—whether through the Quran, your circumstances, or your heart—by insisting it always fits your expectations?

Basran Mu'tazila on Divine Will: Three Impossibilities and Refutation
Basran Mu'tazila affirm a created will not in a locus, incurring three impossibilities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Basran Mu'tazila view on God's will as an originated accident not in a locus, and how the Ash'ari refutes it by demonstrating three impossibilities and additional logical contradictions.

Core Idea
A will not in a locus leads to absurdities that undermine the Mu'tazila own principles.

The Basran Mu'tazila attempted to preserve divine transcendence by denying that God's will subsists in His essence, positing instead a created will that exists without a subject. This lesson unpacks their position and the three impossibilities that follow, along with further refutations showing internal inconsistency and infinite regress. Understanding this debate sharpens one's grasp of how kalam critiques alternative theological models.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Basran position
Read the opening statement: they say God is willing by an originated will not in a locus. This means the will is a created accident that does not reside in any substrate.
Contrast with the Ash'ari view that God's will is an eternal attribute subsisting in His essence.
2
Learn the first impossibility
The first is that originated states would occur upon the Eternal, implying change in God and leading to His origination. This is a classic argument against accidents in God.
3
Learn the second impossibility
The second is that the meaning (the will) would subsist by itself, which is impossible for an accident; it must inhere in a substance.
This violates the fundamental principle of kalam that accidents cannot exist independently.
4
Learn the third impossibility
The third is that the ruling of the will (making God 'willing') would return to a subject in which the will does not subsist, while also denying specificity. This is contradictory.
If the will is not in God, how can it produce the attribute of being 'willing' in God?
5
Reflect on the additional objections
The author adds two more problems: contradiction with their own principle of negating attributes of meanings (they affirm a will as a meaning), and infinite regress (if the will is originated, it needs another will, ad infinitum).
These strengthen the refutation by showing internal inconsistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مذهب معتزلة البصرة في الإرادة واستثنى معتزلة البصرة أيضاً كونَهُ جلَّ وعلا مريداً ، فقالوا : مريد بإرادة حادثة لا في محلّ ، فالتزموا في ذلك ثلاثة أمورٍ كلُّها مستحيلة : أحدها : تجدُّدُ الأحوالِ الحادثة على الأزلي جلَّ وعلا ، وذلكَ يفضي إلى حدوثِ مَنْ وجبَ قدمُهُ ، وقد تقدَّمَ بسْطُ ذلك في حدوثِ العالم (٢) الثاني : قيام المعنى بنفسِهِ ، وهو محال الثالثُ : عود حكمِهِ إلى ما لم يقم بهِ معَ نفْيِ اختصاصِهِ بهِ وهو محال . والزموا أيضاً : مخالفة أصلهم في نفي صفاتِ المعاني ؛ مِنْ حيثُ لم يقولوا : مريد لنفسِهِ كما قالوا : قادرٌ لنفسه وعالم لنفسه ، بل بإرادة ، فأجابوا بالفَرْقِ ؛ قالوا : إنَّهُ لو كانَ مريداً لنفسه كما نقولُ : عالم مثلاً لنفسِهِ .. لعمَّ بمريديَّتِهِ كلَّ ممكن ، وأصلُهم : خروج كثير مِنَ الممكناتِ ـ كالمعاصي ونحوها ـ عن كونها مرادة الله ، تعالى أن يكون في ملكه ما لا يريد ، وما تخيَّلوه في ذلك باطل ؛ إذْ إرادته تعالى عامة التعلقِ بكل ممكن على ما يأتي برهانه (٢) وتحكمهم بأنَّ النفسي هو الذي يعم . . لا يخفى فساده ، وهم قد نقضوه في القادريَّةِ ؛ فإنَّهم زعموا أنَّه تعالى قادر لنفسِهِ ، مع أنَّ أفعال العبادِ الاختياريَّة غيرُ مقدورةٍ عندَهم للهِ ، تعالى عما يقولُ الظالمونَ علوّاً كبيراً . وأيضاً يلزمهم في حدوثِ الإرادة : التسلسل ؛ مِنْ حيث إنَّها حادث مختص بوجود بدلاً عن العدم، وزمان معيَّن بدلاً عن غيره ، فتفتقر إلى إرادة حادثةٍ كما افتقرَتْ سائر الحوادثِ إليها ، ثم ننقل الكلام (٢)
The doctrine of the Basran Mu'tazila concerning will: And the Basran Mu'tazila also excepted His being, glorified and exalted, willing (muridan), so they said: [He is] willing with an originated will (irada haditha) not in a locus. So they incurred in that three matters, all of which are impossible: First: the occurrence of originated states upon the Eternal, glorified and exalted, and that leads to the origination of Him whom pre-eternity is necessary, and the exposition of that has already preceded in the origination of the world (2). Second: the subsistence of the meaning (ma'na) by itself, and that is impossible. Third: the return of its ruling (hukm) to that in which it did not subsist, along with the negation of its being specific to it, and that is impossible. And they are also obliged [to accept] contradiction of their principle in negating attributes of meanings (sifat al-ma'ani), in that they did not say: [He is] willing by Himself as they said: powerful by Himself and knowing by Himself, but rather by a will. So they answered by a distinction; they said: if He were willing by Himself as we say, for example, knowing by Himself... then His will (muridiyya) would encompass every possible thing, and their principle is that many possibles—like sins and the like—are excluded from being willed by Allah. Exalted is Allah above having in His kingdom what He does not will. And what they imagined in that is false; indeed His will, exalted, is general in attachment to every possible, as its proof will come (2). And their arbitrary judgment that the essential (al-nafsi) is what is universal... its falsehood is not hidden, and they have already contradicted it in the case of power (al-qadiriyya); for they claimed that He, exalted, is powerful by Himself, yet the voluntary acts of servants are not within His power according to them. Exalted is Allah above what the wrongdoers say, with a great exaltation. And also there follows upon them in the origination of the will: infinite regress (tasalsul); in that it is an originated thing specific to existence instead of non-existence, and to a particular time instead of another, so it would need another originated will, just as all other originated things need it, then we transfer the discussion (2).
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage presents the Basran Mu'tazila position on God's will (irada) and refutes it with three impossibilities and additional objections. The author first states that the Basran school (followers of Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i) excepted the attribute of will from the general rule that attributes are by essence without meanings. They said God is willing by an originated will (irada haditha) that does not subsist in a locus (i.e., not in God's essence, nor in any other substrate). They thus commit to three absurdities: 1) Accidental origination in the Eternal—if the will is an accident that occurs anew, that would imply change in God, leading to His origination. 2) A meaning (ma'na) subsisting by itself—a will that is not in a subject is impossible because accidents cannot exist independently. 3) The return of the ruling of that will (the effect of making God 'willing') to a subject in which the will does not subsist, while denying that the will is specific to that subject—this is contradictory and impossible. The author then adds further objections: the Basran position contradicts their own principle of negating attributes of meanings (sifat al-ma'ani), because they affirm a will as a meaning. Their reply that if God were willing by Himself, His will would encompass everything, including sins, is invalid because in fact God's will is universal (as will be proven later). Their claim that the essential (nafsi) attribute is universal is refuted by their own view on power (qadiriyya): they say God is powerful by Himself, yet they deny His power over human voluntary acts—so they contradict themselves. Finally, if the will is originated, it would require an infinite regress (tasalsul) because every originated thing needs a cause, and that cause would itself need an originated will, ad infinitum.

Key Terms
الإرادة (al-irada)
المعتزلة البصرة (Mu'tazila al-Basra)
حادثة (haditha)
لا في محل (la fi mahall)
تجدّد الأحوال (tajaddud al-ahwal)
قيام المعنى بنفسه (qiyam al-ma'na bi-nafsihi)
عود الحكم (awd al-hukm)
النفسي (al-nafsi)
التسلسل (al-tasalsul)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Basran Mu'tazila deny God has a will entirely.
Correction: They affirm God is willing by an originated will not in a locus; they do not deny will altogether, but hold a different ontological status.

Checkpoint
What is the third impossibility (al-muhal) that the author says follows from the Basran Mu'tazila view on divine will?
A) The occurrence of originated states upon the Eternal
B) The subsistence of the meaning by itself
C) The return of its ruling to that in which it did not subsist, with negation of specificity
D) The infinite regress of wills

Key Takeaway
The Basran Mu'tazila view that God's will is an originated accident not in a locus is refuted by three logical impossibilities and further objections, demonstrating the necessity of affirming an eternal will subsisting in God's essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that Allah's will encompasses every event, including your sins and failures? How does that conviction affect your repentance and hope in His mercy, or does it make you complacent?
?
The Basran Mu'tazila separated God's will from His essence to avoid attributing evil to Him. Are there any attributes of Allah that you find uncomfortable and try to reinterpret to fit your own sense of justice? What does that reveal about your trust in His wisdom?
?
This passage shows that denying an eternal will in God leads to logical absurdities. Have you ever, even in small ways, treated Allah's will as if it were limited or separate from His essence? How can you actively affirm His complete sovereignty over your life starting today?

Views of al-Ka'bi and al-Najjar on Divine Will
Sanusi presents and refutes Mu'tazili interpretations of divine will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazili reinterpretations of divine will by al-Ka'bi and al-Najjar, and why Sanusi rejects them by referencing the earlier proof for the attribute of will.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazili denial of will as a positive attribute fails because it contradicts the earlier proof.

In this passage, Sanusi addresses two Mu'tazili scholars who deny that will is a positive attribute of Allah. Al-Ka'bi interprets 'willing' as creating and commanding, while al-Najjar reduces it to a negative attribute meaning not coerced. Sanusi dismisses both by pointing back to the earlier demonstration that Allah truly has an attribute of will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, noting the two different interpretations of divine will presented by al-Ka'bi and al-Najjar.
Al-Ka'bi and al-Najjar both deny will as a positive attribute but differ in their reinterpretations.
2
Compare the two reinterpretations
Identify how al-Ka'bi and al-Najjar each explain 'willing'. Notice that al-Najjar turns an existential attribute into a negative one.
Al-Najjar's view specifically interprets will as a negative attribute, which is a more extreme redefinition.
3
Understand the refutation
The author refutes both views by referencing the earlier proof that establishes the reality of divine will. This shows that the refutation relies on previously established proof, not a new argument.
The refutation is concise: the same proof that affirmed will as an attribute disproves these reinterpretations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مذهب الكعبي والنجار في الإرادة ] وذهب الكعبي والنجَّارُ وأتباعُهما إلى إنكار هذه الصفة أصلاً وتأوَّلوا كونه مريداً لما ورد السمعُ بإطلاقِهِ ، فقال الكعبي : معناه بالنسبة إلى أفعالِهِ : أَنَّهُ خالقها ومنشتُها ، وبالنسبة إلى أفعال عبيده : أَنَّهُ آمر بها ، وقال النجارُ : معنى كونِهِ مريداً : أَنَّهُ غيرُ مغلوب ولا مستكره ، وفسَّرَ الصفة الوجوديَّة المتعلقة .. بصفة سلبية لا تعلُّق لها أصلاً بغيرِ مَن اتَّصفَ بها ، والدليل على ردّ هذا المذهب الدليل على ثبوت كونه تعالى مريداً ، وقد تقدَّمَ
[The school of al-Ka'bi and al-Najjar regarding will]. Al-Ka'bi and al-Najjar and their followers went to the denial of this attribute entirely and reinterpreted His being 'willing' as it occurs in the transmitted texts absolutely. Al-Ka'bi said: Its meaning with respect to His actions is that He is their creator and originator, and with respect to the actions of His servants is that He commands them. Al-Najjar said: The meaning of His being willing is that He is not overcome nor coerced. And he interpreted the existential attribute that is related... as a negative attribute that has no relation at all to other than the one described by it. The proof for refuting this school is the proof for the affirmation that He, Exalted, is willing, and it has preceded.
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage presents the views of two Mu'tazili theologians, al-Ka'bi and al-Najjar, who deny will as a positive attribute of Allah. Al-Ka'bi interprets 'willing' as creator and commander, while al-Najjar interprets it as not being coerced (a negative attribute). The author refutes them by referring to the earlier proof for the existence of the attribute of will. This is a sectarian discussion and refutation passage.

Key Terms

إنكار
تأويل
صفة سلبية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that reinterpreting will as a negative attribute (e.g., not coerced) is acceptable and preserves tawhid.
Correction: The author rejects both reinterpretations outright because the earlier proof established will as a positive existential attribute; reducing it to a negative or functional interpretation contradicts that proof.

Checkpoint

A) As a positive existential attribute.
B) As a negative attribute meaning not being overcome or coerced.
C) As His being the creator of actions.
D) As His commanding actions.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ka'bi and al-Najjar both denied will as a positive attribute, but Sanusi refutes them by appealing to the established proof that Allah truly possesses the attribute of will.

Philosophers and Mu'tazila on Divine Attributes
Sanusi describes the philosophers' denial of all divine attributes and Mu'tazila adoption.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' denial of all positive attributes for Allah and their three categories of acceptable descriptions, as well as how the Mu'tazila adopted their views with modifications.

Core Idea
Philosophers reduce divine attributes to mere negations, relations, and composites—a path later followed by the Mu'tazila.

Sanusi now turns to a sect whose denial of divine attributes goes further than the earlier groups. The philosophers (falasifa) reject every positive attribute of Allah, allowing only empty categories: negation, relation, or a mix. This passage also reveals the Mu'tazila's alignment with them, prompting the author's prayer for protection from such misguidance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the structure: the philosophers' position, their three categories, and the author's conclusion.
The footnote numeral has been removed from the text.
2
Identify the three categories
The philosophers allow only three ways to describe Allah: by negation (e.g., intellect detached from matter), by relation (e.g., principle), or by a composite of negation and relation (e.g., generous = gives without stinginess). List them in your own words.
Each category is a type of 'attribute' but empties it of real positive content.
3
Observe the Mu'tazila's connection
Sanusi notes that the Mu'tazila followed the philosophers' path with modifications. Reflect on what this implies: a chain of deviation from the Sunni understanding of attributes.
4
Understand the author's response
The passage ends with a prayer seeking refuge from trials and for steadfastness on the Sunnah. This shows the gravity of the error in the author's view.
The prayer is not just a formality; it emphasizes the danger of these views.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مذهب الفلاسفة في الإرادة ] هو وأما الفلاسفةُ : فأنكروا صفاتِ الباري تعالى كلها ، قالوا : ولا يتّصفُ إلا بسلب ؛ كتسميتهم له عاقلاً لذاته ، ومعنى عقليَّتِهِ لذاتِهِ عندهم : تجرُّدُهُ عنِ المادة ، أو بإضافة ؛ كتسميتهم له مبدأ ، أو بقضيَّةٍ مُركَّبَةٍ مِنْ سَلْبٍ وإضافةٍ ، كتسميتهم له جواداً ، ومعناه : أَنَّهُ يعطي مِنْ غيرِ بخل . وقد سلكتِ المعتزلة آثارهم بتغيير ما ، نعوذُ باللهِ مِنَ الفتنِ المضلة ، والأهواء المردية ، وأحيانا الله وأماتنا على اتباع السنَّةِ وأنالَنا مِنْ عصمتِهِ وتوفيقه ما يكون لنا في الدنيا والآخرةِ أعظمَ جُنَّةِ آمين يا ربَّ العالمينَ
[The school of the philosophers regarding will]. As for the philosophers, they denied all attributes of the Creator, Exalted is He. They said: He is only qualified by negation; like their calling Him 'intellect of His essence', and the meaning of His being intellect of His essence according to them is His detachment from matter; or by relation, like their calling Him 'principle'; or by a composite statement of negation and relation, like their calling Him 'generous', meaning He gives without stinginess. And the Mu'tazila followed their path with some modification. We seek refuge with Allah from misleading trials and destructive whims. May Allah keep us and cause us to die upon following the Sunnah and grant us from His protection and success what will be for us in this world and the Hereafter the greatest shield. Amen, O Lord of the worlds.
Source pages p. 312

Commentary
This passage describes the philosophers' (falasifa) denial of all positive attributes for Allah. They only allow negative attributes (like being intellect of His essence meaning detachment from matter), relational attributes (like being the first principle), or composites of negation and relation (like being generous, meaning He gives without stinginess). The author then notes that the Mu'tazila followed the philosophers' path with modifications, and concludes with a prayer for protection from misguidance and for adherence to the Sunnah. This is a sectarian discussion passage.

Key Terms

سلب
إضافة
تجرُّد عن المادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers' denial of attributes is a form of tanzih (transcendence) acceptable in Islam.
Correction: Sanusi condemns it as misguidance: they reduce Allah to mere negations and relations, stripping Him of real attributes affirmed by revelation.

Checkpoint

A) He has the attribute of generosity.
B) He is detached from matter.
C) He is the first principle.
D) He gives without stinginess.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers deny all positive attributes, allowing only negations, relations, or composites—a view the Mu'tazila later adopted with modifications, and which Sanusi warns against.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Correction: Christians not disbelieved for affirming attributes
Sanusi corrects the mistaken claim that Christians were deemed disbelievers for affirming attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific reason Christians were declared disbelievers, and why affirming attributes alone does not necessitate disbelief.

Core Idea
Affirming attributes is not disbelief; it is the claim of multiple gods that is rejected.

A common argument is that Christians were declared disbelievers because they affirmed attributes like essence, knowledge, and life. Sanusi corrects this, showing that the Qur'anic condemnation is for tritheism, not for affirming attributes. This lesson clarifies the distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the erroneous claim
The passage begins with a common mistaken claim. Find it and note that Sanusi calls it an error.
2
Understand the correction
Read Sanusi's correction and the Qur'anic evidence he provides. Notice he explicitly states the reason is affirming three gods.
3
Reflect on the distinction
Consider why affirming attributes is different from claiming multiple gods. This distinction is crucial for aqeedah.
4
Connect to earlier content
Sanusi references a previous explanation of the Christian statement. Recall how earlier lessons described their position.

وأما قولهم : كُفِّرَتِ النصارى بإثباتِهم الذات والعلم والحياة) فخطأ؛ إذ لم يكن تكفيرهم بمُجرَّدِ إثباتِ ذلكَ ، بل بإثباتهم آلهة ثلاثة ؛ على ما قال تعالى: ﴿لَقَدْ كَفَرَ الَّذِينَ قَالُوا إِنَّ اللَّهَ ثَالِثُ ثَلَاثَةِ ﴾ [المائدة : ٧٣]، وقد قدَّمنا شرحَ مقالتهم التي لا يرضى بها مُميَّز مِنَ الصبيانِ فضلاً عمَّنْ فوقه

[5:73]. And we have previously presented an explanation of their statement with which even a distinguishing child would not be pleased, let alone those above him.
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal correction. He clarifies that the real reason for their disbelief was their belief in three gods (tritheism), citing Qur'an 5:73. He then notes that their absurd position was previously explained in a way that even a discerning child would reject. The passage follows the author's order: first the erroneous claim, then the correction with scriptural evidence, then a reminder of prior explanation.
