Refuting the Mu'tazilite Claim on the More Specific and More General in Similarity
Sanusi refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that sharing in the more specific necessitates sharing in the more general.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazilite claim that sharing in the more specific necessitates sharing in the more general is false, and how Sanusi's counterexamples show that the specific only implies the general.

Core Idea
Sharing in the more specific does not cause sharing in the more general; it only implies it.

In this passage, Sanusi challenges the Mu'tazilite definition of similarity. The Mu'tazilites argued that two things are similar if they share the most specific essential attribute, and that this specific sharing is the cause of their sharing in more general attributes. Sanusi objects with a logical argument and clear counterexamples, showing that the general can exist without the specific.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the structure: Sanusi first states his objection, then explains the Mu'tazilite position, then gives his proof.
The Arabic ends abruptly at a page break, but the argument is complete in meaning.
2
Identify the two opposing claims
Identify what the Mu'tazilites claim (sharing in the specific is a cause for sharing in the general) and what Sanusi claims (it is only an implication, not a cause).
3
Analyze the counterexamples
Examine the examples of horse/man and whiteness/blackness. How do they prove that the general can exist without the specific?
4
Understand the logical distinction
Focus on the terms 'cause' (علة) and 'implication' (ملزوم). Sanusi argues that the specific is a malzūm (that which implies) for the general, not a cause.
This distinction is key in kalam reasoning about similarity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ الإيجاب للأخص في باب التماثل ممتنع ] ثم الإيجابُ للأخص في بابِ التماثل ممتنع ؛ لوجودِ الاشتراك فى الأعم مع انتفائِهِ في الأخص هذا اعتراض على المعتزلة في قولهم : إنَّ الاشتراك في الأخص يُوجِبُ الاشتراك في الأعم ؛ أي: هو عِلَّةٌ له ، ولهذا قالوا : حقيقة المثلين : هما المشتركان في الأخصّ ، واشتراكهما في الأخصّ عِلَّةٌ لاشتراكهما في الأعم وتقرير الاعتراض عليهم : أنَّ الاشتراك في الأخصّ لو كانَ مُوجِباً للاشتراك في الأعم ؛ أي : عِلَّةً له كما زعموا . . لَمَا وُجِدَ الاشتراك في الأعم بدونِ الاشتراكِ في الأخصّ ؛ لاستحالة وجودِ المعلول بدونِ علتِهِ ، لكن التالي باطل ، فالمقدَّمُ مثلُهُ ودليل بطلانه : أنَّ الفرس والإنسان مشتركان في الأعم الذاتي وهو الحيوانية ، وليسا مشتركين في الأخصّ ؛ كالناطقية أو الصاهليةِ ، وكذا البياضُ والسواد يشتركان في اللونية وهي ذاتي أعم ، ولا يشتركان في الأخص وهو السوادية أو البياضية وإنَّما الواجبُ أَنْ يُقالَ : الاشتراك في الأخص الذاتي ملزوم للاشتراك في الأعم الذاتي ، فيلزمُ مِنْ وجودِ الاشتراك في الأخص وجود
[The necessitation of the more specific in the chapter of similarity is impossible] Then the necessitation of the more specific in the chapter of similarity is impossible, because there exists sharing in the more general while it is absent in the more specific. This is an objection against the Mu'tazilites in their saying: Sharing in the more specific necessitates sharing in the more general; i.e., it is a cause for it. For this reason they said: The reality of two similars (mithlayn) is that they are the two that share in the more specific, and their sharing in the more specific is a cause for their sharing in the more general. And the exposition of the objection against them: If sharing in the more specific were necessitating sharing in the more general, i.e., a cause for it as they claim, then sharing in the more general would not exist without sharing in the more specific, because it is impossible for an effect to exist without its cause. But the consequent is false, so the antecedent is likewise. And the proof of its falsehood: that the horse and man share in the more general essential (al-aʿamm al-dhātī), which is animality, and they do not share in the more specific, like rationality (nāṭiqiyya) or neighing (ṣāḥiliyya). Likewise, whiteness and blackness share in color-ness (lawniyya), which is an essential more general, and they do not share in the more specific, which is blackness (sawādiyya) or whiteness (bayāḍiyya). And rather it is necessary to say: Sharing in the essential more specific implies (malzūm li) sharing in the essential more general, so from the existence of sharing in the more specific follows the existence [of sharing in the more general, but not vice versa].
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer against the Mu'tazilite theory of similarity. The Mu'tazilites held that two things are similar (mithlān) if they share in the most specific essential attribute, and that sharing in that specific attribute is the cause for sharing in the more general attributes. Sanusi objects that if the specific were a necessary cause for the general, then the general could never exist without the specific. But counterexamples exist: a horse and a human share in animality (the more general) without sharing in rationality or neighing (the more specific). Similarly, whiteness and blackness share in color (general) without sharing in the specific color essences. Therefore, the specific is not a cause but only an implication (luzūm): if the specific exists, the general necessarily exists, but the general can exist without the specific. The passage thus refutes the Mu'tazilite definition of similarity and their causal claim.

Key Terms
إيجاب للأخص
تماثل
اشتراك في الأعم
اشتراك في الأخص
ذاتي
علة
ملزوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that sharing in the more specific is a cause (ʿilla) for sharing in the more general.
Correction: Sanusi shows it is only an implication (luzūm): the specific implies the general, but the general can exist without the specific.

Checkpoint
What counterexample does Sanusi give to refute the Mu'tazilite claim that sharing in the more specific necessitates sharing in the more general?
A) Horse and man share in animality but not in rationality or neighing
B) Whiteness and blackness share in color but not in shape
C) Man and angel share in rationality but not in animality
D) Fire and water share in heat and coldness

Key Takeaway
The more specific essential attribute implies the more general, but not vice versa; therefore the Mu'tazilite causal claim is false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that because two things are similar in one specific way, they must be similar in every broader way? When? How might this lesson protect you from overgeneralizing in your understanding of Allah's creation and attributes?
?
The Mu'tazilites turned a logical implication into a causal necessity, leading to error. Is there an area in your own life where you have insisted that a certain practice or belief must necessarily lead to a specific outcome? How can you submit to Allah's greater wisdom instead?
?
If sharing in the more specific is not required for similarity, then what truly makes two things similar in Allah's sight? How does this affect the way you view fellow believers who differ from you in specific details but share the general core of faith?

Refuting the Hashwiyyah on Eternal Speech
Sanusi refutes the Hashwiyyah on eternal speech

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Hashwiyyah's claim that God's speech is eternal letters and sounds contradicts necessary knowledge and is therefore rationally impossible.

Core Idea
Letters and sounds are inherently temporal; calling them eternal destroys reason.

In this passage, Sanusi targets a literalist sect known as the Hashwiyyah, who claimed that God's speech, while subsisting in His essence, consists of letters and sounds and yet is eternal. This might sound plausible to some, but Sanusi argues it is the height of ignorance. He contrasts them with other heretics who at least maintain some rational sense, while the Hashwiyyah blatantly contradict self-evident truths.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the strong condemnatory language: 'utmost extreme of misguidance'.
2
Identify the Hashwiyyah's claim
Find the exact statement: they say God's speech is letters and sounds AND it is pre-eternal (qadīm azalī). Mark this as the core assertion being refuted.
3
Understand the refutation
Sanusi says they violated 'necessary knowledge' (ḍarūrāt). Ask yourself: why are letters and sounds necessarily temporal? Consider that letters require sequence, sounds require air, etc.
Contrast with 'fanciful doubts' that other innovators might have.
4
Connect to larger theology
Reflect on why this matters: if God's speech is eternal, it cannot be composed of parts like letters. This safeguards divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذهبَتِ الحشوية المنتمون إلى الظاهر : إلى أنَّ كلام الباري تعالى القائم بذاتِهِ حروفٌ وأصوات ، ومع كونه حروفاً وأصواتاً هو قديم أزلي ! وهؤلاء أصحابُ غايةٍ في الضلالةِ ، وتورُّط في بحبوحة الجهالة ، فإِنَّ مَنْ سواهم مِنْ أهل البدع ربَّما تعنُّ لهم شبهةٌ مُخَيَّلَةٌ لا تَهْدِمُ مِنْ أَوَّلِ مَرَّةٍ الضرورات (۲) ، أما هؤلاء فلم يراعوا ضروراتِ
And the Hashwiyyah, who attribute themselves to the literal [school], went to the view that the speech of the Creator, may He be exalted, subsisting in His essence, is letters and sounds, and despite being letters and sounds, it is pre-eternal and eternal! These are people at the utmost extreme of misguidance and immersed in the midst of ignorance. For indeed, others among the people of innovation may sometimes be presented with a fanciful doubt that does not destroy necessary [knowledge] from the first time, but these [Hashwiyyah] did not observe the necessary [knowledge].
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Hashwiyyah (a group of anthropomorphists) who claim that God's eternal speech subsisting in His essence is composed of letters and sounds, yet is pre-eternal (qadīm azalī). The author declares them to be in extreme misguidance and immersed in ignorance. He contrasts them with other innovators (ahl al-bidaʿ) who may occasionally have a fanciful doubt that does not immediately overturn necessary knowledge (ḍarūrāt). But the Hashwiyyah, he says, did not even observe necessary knowledge—meaning they contradicted the self-evident truth that letters and sounds are temporal. The passage is incomplete but clearly condemns their position as absurd.

Key Terms
حشوية
كلام
قديم أزلي
حروف
أصوات
ضرورات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if God's speech is eternal, it could be letters and sounds, since letters and sounds are not inherently temporal in God's case.
Correction: The passage argues that letters and sounds are necessarily temporal because they involve sequence and composition, which are incompatible with eternity. The Hashwiyyah's claim violates necessary knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the error of the Hashwiyyah?
A) They denied that God speaks.
B) They claimed that God's speech is composed of letters and sounds yet is eternal.
C) They said God's speech is created.
D) They denied that speech subsists in God's essence.

Key Takeaway
The Hashwiyyah's claim that God's speech is eternal letters and sounds contradicts the self-evident truth that letters and sounds are temporal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief that seemed 'literal' but contradicted something you know to be true by necessity? How do you reconcile that? What will you do today to ensure you are not ignoring clear reasoning in your faith?
?
This passage shows that the Hashwiyyah ignored self-evident truths. In your own life, are there areas where you might be clinging to an interpretation without checking if it violates basic reason? Ask Allah for guidance to accept truth even when it is uncomfortable.
?
How does the fact that letters and sounds are temporal deepen your understanding of the Qur'an as God's eternal speech? Does this change how you approach recitation or reflection on the Qur'an? Commit to one change in your relationship with the Qur'an starting today.

The Three Ignorances of the Hashwiyya
Sanusi refutes the Hashwiyya claim about divine power and enumerates their three ignorances.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three ignorances of the Hashwiyya and why their literalist approach leads to theological impossibility and disbelief.

Core Idea
Literalism without language mastery and intellect leads to theological absurdity.

Sanusi exposes a fundamental error of the Hashwiyya: their claim about divine power forces an impossible dilemma. He then identifies three root ignorances that underpin their entire approach to scripture and reason. This passage not only refutes a specific sect but teaches a vital lesson about the proper use of language, intellect, and theological methodology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dilemma
Read how the Hashwiyya's claim about divine power leads to a dilemma: either God can create another god (disbelief) or He is incapable (negates divinity).
This dilemma shows their reasoning is self-defeating.
2
Understand the first ignorance
The first ignorance is ignorance of language and the difference between literal and metaphorical meanings. This leads them to take anthropomorphic verses literally.
Examples: istiwā', nuzūl, Quran in hearts, God's voice.
3
See the second ignorance
The second ignorance is rigidity—they stubbornly adhere to the literal meanings inherited from their predecessors without reflection.
4
Recognize the third ignorance
The third ignorance is sophistry against the intellect—they twist reason to avoid abandoning the literal meaning.
They reject sound rational arguments out of fear of leaving literalism.
5
Grasp the consequence
These three ignorances, combined with presumptuousness and lack of proper learning, are the root of all misguidance and disbelief.
This emphasizes the need for proper linguistic and intellectual training.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تركب مذهب الحشوية من جهالات ثلاث وزعموا أنَّ القديمَ سبحانَهُ لو لم يُوصَفْ بالاقتدار على ذلك لكانَ عاجزاً، وذلكَ منهم جهل بما يتعلَّقُ بهِ الاقتدار والعجز، ويلزمهم على هذا أن يكونَ سبحانَهُ قادراً على اختراع إلـه مثلِهِ له، فإن امتنعوا مِنْ ذلكَ أُلزموا كونَهُ عاجزاً على مقتضى رأيهم، والعاجز ليس بإلله، وإن حكموا باقتدارِهِ على ذلكَ لزمهم مِنَ الكَفْرِ ما لزمَ مَنْ قالَ بوجودِ مثلِ اللهِ تعالى؛ إذْ لا فرق في الكفرِ بينَ مَنْ يُجوِّزُ في حقٌّ الله تعالى ما يقدح في أُلوهِيَّتِهِ، وبينَ مَنْ يحكم بوقوع ذلكَ. فتركَّبَ مذهبُ الحشويَّةِ مِنْ ثلاث جهالات: إحداها: جهلُهم باللسان والفرق بين مجازه وحقيقته، ولهذا حكموا بظاهرِ ما وردَ مِنَ الاستواء على العرشِ، والنزول إلى السماء في الثلث الآخرِ مِنَ الليلِ، وكون القرآن كلام الله محفوظاً الصدور، مقروءاً بالألسن، مكتوباً في المصاحفِ، وما وردَ مِنْ نداء الله تعالى في الآخرة بصوت يسمعُهُ مَنْ قَرُبَ ومَنْ بَعُدَ، وغير ذلكَ ممَّا لا يُحصى. الثانية: جمودهم على ما سبق إليهم مِنْ ظاهرِ اللفظ. والثالثة: مغالطتهم العقول حذراً مِنْ تركِ الظاهر. ولا شكّ أنَّ الجهل باللسان، وعدم إتقان فنّي البلاغة والبيانِ والبعدَ مِنْ ممارسة العلوم العقليَّةِ، على مقتضى التنبيهات الشرعيَّة، ثم التجاسر - معَ عدمِ ذلك كله ـ على الخوض فيما يحتاج إلى علوم عديدة وفكرةٍ متقدمة وتأييد إلهي، مِنْ غيرِ أَخْذ عن أهل العلم، وحسنِ أدب في التلقّي منهم.. أصل لكل ضلالةٍ وكفر والعياذ بالله.
The doctrine of the Hashwiyya is composed of three ignorances. They claimed that the Eternal, glory be to Him, if He were not described as having power over that, He would be incapable. This is ignorance on their part of what power and incapability pertain to. It follows from this, according to them, that He, glory be to Him, would be capable of inventing a god like Himself. If they refrain from that, they are compelled to hold Him incapable according to their view, and the incapable is not a god. And if they judge that He has power over that, then they incur the same disbelief as one who says that there exists a like of Allah Almighty; for there is no difference in disbelief between one who permits regarding Allah Almighty what impugns His divinity, and one who judges that it occurs. So the doctrine of the Hashwiyya is composed of three ignorances: The first: their ignorance of language and the difference between its metaphor and its literal meaning, and therefore they judged by the literal meaning of what came regarding being established over the Throne, and descending to the heaven in the last third of the night, and that the Quran is the speech of Allah preserved in breasts, recited by tongues, written in copies, and what came regarding the call of Allah Almighty in the Hereafter with a voice that those near and far hear, and other things too numerous to count. The second: their rigidity on what came to them of the literal meaning. The third: their sophistry against the intellects out of caution against abandoning the literal meaning. And there is no doubt that ignorance of language, lack of mastery of the two arts of rhetoric and eloquence, distance from practicing the intellectual sciences in accordance with the Shari'a alerts, and then presumptuousness - with all that lacking - to delve into what requires numerous sciences, advanced thought, and divine support, without taking from the people of knowledge and good etiquette in receiving from them... is the source of every misguidance and disbelief, and God forbid.
Source pages p. 370

Commentary
This passage is a refutation and classification of the Hashwiyya's errors. The author begins by presenting their claim that if God were not described as having power over a certain thing, He would be incapable. He then points out that this claim is based on ignorance of what power and incapability truly entail. The author argues that their reasoning forces them into a dilemma: either God has power to create another god like Himself (which is absurd) or He is incapable (which negates divinity). If they accept the first, they commit disbelief by positing a like of God; if the second, they deny His divinity. The author then states that the Hashwiyya doctrine is composed of three ignorances. First, ignorance of language and the distinction between literal and metaphorical meanings, leading them to take literally verses about God's attributes such as 'istiwā'' (being established over the Throne), 'nuzūl' (descending), and the Quran being in hearts and written in copies, as well as the voice of God in the Hereafter. Second, rigidity in adhering to literal interpretations inherited from predecessors. Third, sophistry against the intellect out of fear of abandoning the literal meaning. The author concludes that ignorance of language, lack of rhetoric, distance from intellectual sciences within Shari'a bounds, and rushing into deep theological matters without proper learning from scholars is the root of all misguidance and disbelief.

الحشوية (Hashwiyya)
الجهالات (ignorances)
الاقتدار (power)
العجز (incapability)
مقادح الألوهية (impugnments of divinity)
الاستواء (istiwā')
النزول (nuzūl)
مغالطة العقول (sophistry against intellects)
التنبيهات الشرعية (Shari'a alerts)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Hashwiyya's literalism is a simple hermeneutic error
Correction: The author shows it leads to theological impossibility and disbelief, rooted in three deep ignorances.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what theological dilemma does the Hashwiyya's claim about divine power lead to?
A) God must be either capable of creating another god or incapable, both of which are impossible.
B) God is not omnipotent.
C) The Hashwiyya deny God's existence.
D) The Hashwiyya affirm anthropomorphism but avoid disbelief.

Key Takeaway
The Hashwiyya's three ignorances—ignorance of language, rigidity, and sophistry—collectively lead to theological impossibility and disbelief, highlighting the necessity of proper linguistic and rational training in Islamic theology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Where in your own understanding of faith do you cling to a literal interpretation of a text without considering its deeper meaning or context, even when the intellect suggests otherwise? Will you commit to seeking proper scholarly guidance to resolve that tension?
?
When have you been rigid in a belief simply because it was what you were taught, refusing to re-examine it even when evidence or reason pointed to a different understanding? How will you break that rigidity?
?
Are you sometimes guilty of delving into complex theological discussions without the necessary linguistic and rational training, relying on superficial reading or emotion? What specific step will you take today to ensure you learn from qualified scholars with humility?

Hashwiyya Belief Composed of Three Heresies
Sanusi enumerates the three heresies composing Hashwiyya belief and clarifies their divergence from Mu'tazilism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Hashwiyya sect's creed combines elements from three different heretical groups and how they differ from the Mu'tazila on the nature of God's speech.

Core Idea
The Hashwiyya creed is a contradictory patchwork of Jewish, Christian, and Mu'tazilite errors.

In this passage, Sanusi exposes the syncretic nature of the Hashwiyya sect, showing that their beliefs are not merely literalist but actually borrow from three previous heresies: Jewish corporealism, Christian indwelling of speech, and Mu'tazilite literalism about letters and sounds. The key distinction is that the Mu'tazila, unlike the Hashwiyya, did not attribute letters and sounds directly to God's essence. This lesson unpacks the three components and their contradictions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three errors
Read the passage and list the three groups from whom the Hashwiyya borrow: Jews, Christians, Mu'tazila. Notice what specific belief they share with each.
2
Understand the Jewish connection
The Hashwiyya share with the Jews the belief that God has a body (corporealism). This is clearly stated in the passage.
This is a major anthropomorphic heresy.
3
Understand the Christian connection
They share with the Christians the belief in the indwelling (ḥulūl) of speech in bodies and that the Quran as eternal speech is present in creation without separation from God.
This resembles the Christian doctrine of the incarnation of the Logos.
4
Understand the Mu'tazilite connection and the difference
They share with the Mu'tazila the view that God's speech consists of letters and sounds. However, the Mu'tazila deny that these letters and sounds inhere in God's essence because they are created. The Hashwiyya inconsistently affirm both inherence and the letter-sound nature.
This highlights the contradictions.
5
Reflect on the overall lesson
Consider the implications: the Hashwiyya combine incompatible heresies, showing the incoherence of their theology. This passage is a critique of syncretism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالجملة: فاعتقادُ الحشويةِ تألَّفَ مِنْ ضلالاتٍ ثلاث: مِنْ تهودٍ، وتنصُّرٍ، واعتزال؛ فهم معَ اليهود في اعتقادِ الجسم في حقّ الإله، ومع النصارى في اعتقادِ حلول الكلام في الأجسام، وأنَّهُ لا يفارقُ معَ ذلك الإلة، ومع المعتزلة في اعتقادِ أَنَّ كلام الله تعالى حروفٌ وأصوات، وهو نص مذهب اليهود أيضاً، غير أنَّ المعتزلة لم يقولوا بقيام الحروفِ والأصوات به سبحانه وتعالى؛ لما تفطنوا لحدوثها.
In summary: the belief of the Hashwiyya is composed of three errors: from Judaism, from Christianity, and from Mu'tazilism. For they are with the Jews in believing in a body with respect to God, and with the Christians in believing in the indwelling of speech in bodies, and that it does not separate from God along with that, and with the Mu'tazila in believing that the speech of Allah Almighty is letters and sounds, and that is also the express position of the Jews, except that the Mu'tazila did not say that letters and inhere in Him, glory be to Him, because they realized that they are originated.
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This passage classifies the Hashwiyya's belief as a composite of three prior heresies. The author states that their belief is composed of errors from Judaism, Christianity, and Mu'tazilism. With the Jews, they share the belief in a body (corporealism) regarding God. With the Christians, they share the belief in the indwelling of speech in bodies (i.e., the Quran being in physical objects like copies and hearts) and that it does not separate from God (i.e., it is uncreated and present in creation). With the Mu'tazila, they share the belief that God's speech is letters and sounds. The author notes that this belief in letters and sounds is also the explicit position of the Jews. However, the Mu'tazila differ from the Hashwiyya in that they did not affirm that letters and sounds inhere in God's essence, because they recognized that letters and sounds are originated (creatures) and thus cannot be attributed to God. Thus the Hashwiyya combine contradictory heresies.

Key Terms
تهود (Judaism)
تنصر (Christianity)
اعتزال (Mu'tazilism)
الجسم (body/corporealism)
حلول الكلام (indwelling of speech)
حروف وأصوات (letters and sounds)
قيام الحروف (inherence of letters)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Hashwiyya are merely extreme literalists who take scripture literally.
Correction: The passage shows they actually adopt specific heretical doctrines from Judaism (corporealism), Christianity (indwelling speech), and Mu'tazilism (letter-sound theory), resulting in a contradictory composite, not simple literalism.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, with which group do the Hashwiyya share the belief that God's speech is composed of letters and sounds?
A) Only the Jews
B) Only the Mu'tazila
C) Both the Jews and the Mu'tazila
D) The Christians and the Jews

Key Takeaway
The Hashwiyya creed is an incoherent blend of corporealism, indwelling speech, and letter-sound literalism, drawn from three different heretical traditions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think about your own beliefs regarding God's attributes. Are there any that you hold simply because they are 'common' or 'familiar' without checking their consistency with orthodox theology? How would you respond if you discovered a borrowed error in your own creed?
?
The Hashwiyya combined elements from three different heresies without realizing the contradictions. Do you ever accept teachings from different sources without examining their coherence? What steps can you take to ensure your faith is not a patchwork of inconsistencies?
?
If someone today claimed that the Quran is a combination of created letters and sounds yet also eternal, how would you refute them using the arguments from this lesson? More importantly, does your own heart rest on the belief that God's speech is an eternal attribute, or do you unknowingly treat it as temporal?

The Hashwiyya's Judaizing and Christianizing Deviations
Sanusi presents the first two components of the Hashwiyya heresy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Hashwiyya sect incorporated Jewish anthropomorphism and Christian incarnationism into their creed.

Core Idea
The Hashwiyya combined Jewish body-belief and Christian speech-incarnation.

Sanusi begins his critique of the Hashwiyya by exposing their syncretic origins. He shows that their doctrine is not original but a patchwork of earlier heresies. In this lesson, we focus on the first two components: their adoption of a corporeal God from Judaism and their belief in the indwelling of divine speech from Christianity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage identifying the two deviations
Read the Arabic passage carefully and note how Sanusi explicitly links the Hashwiyya to Jewish and Christian beliefs.
Pay attention to the words 'مع اليهود' and 'مع النصارى'.
2
Compare with orthodox Islamic belief
Reflect on why these positions are considered deviations in Islam: God is not a body, and His speech does not dwell in physical entities.
The orthodox view rejects both anthropomorphism and incarnation.
3
Reflect on the term 'deviation'
Consider why Sanusi calls these borrowings 'ضلالات' (deviations) rather than simply errors.
The term implies a departure from the true path.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالجملة: فاعتقاد الحشوية تألف من ضلالات ثلاث: من تهود، وتنصر، واعتزال؛ فهم مع اليهود في اعتقاد الجسم في حق الإله، ومع النصارى في اعتقاد حلول الكلام في الأجسام، وأنه لا يفارق مع ذلك الإله، ومع المعتزلة في اعتقاد أن كلام الله تعالى حروف وأصوات، وهو نص مذهب اليهود أيضاً، غير أن المعتزلة لم يقولوا بقيام الحروف والأصوات به سبحانه وتعالى؛ لما تفطنوا لحدوثها، وأدركوا أن قيام الحوادث بذاته تعالى محال، وهؤلاء حكموا بذلك لعظيم غباوتهم وجهلهم الضروريات التي تدرك بأوائل العقول واشترك الجميع في عدم تعقل ما قاله أهل الحق من إثبات كلام ليس بحرف ولا صوت، قائم بنفس المتكلم، يعبر عنه بالكلام اللفظي والكتابة والرموز والإشارات.
In summary: the creed of the Hashwiyya is composed of three deviations: from Judaism, from Christianity, and from Mu'tazilism. They are with the Jews in believing in a body in respect of the deity, with the Christians in believing in the indwelling of speech in bodies and that it does not separate from the deity, and with the Mu'tazila in believing that the speech of Allah Most High is letters and sounds—and that is also the very doctrine of the Jews—except that the Mu'tazila did not affirm the subsistence of letters and sounds in Him, may He be exalted and glorified, because they realized their temporality and perceived that the subsistence of temporal accidents in His essence is impossible. These [Hashwiyya] ruled otherwise due to their immense stupidity and ignorance of the necessary truths that are grasped by the primary intellects. And all of them share in failing to comprehend what the People of Truth affirmed: the affirmation of speech that is neither letter nor sound, subsisting in the self of the speaker, expressed by verbal speech, writing, symbols, and indications.
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This passage classifies the doctrine of the Hashwiyya (anthropomorphists) as a syncretistic heresy composed of three distinct deviations. Sanusi first states that their creed combines elements from Judaism, Christianity, and Mu'tazilism. He then breaks down each component: they agree with the Jews in attributing a body to God; with the Christians in believing that speech incarnates or dwells in bodies and that it never departs from the divine essence (thus implying incarnation); and with the Mu'tazila in holding that God's speech consists of letters and sounds. He notes that this is also the explicit position of the Jews. However, Sanusi highlights a key difference: the Mu'tazila, due to their rational acumen, realized that letters and sounds are temporal accidents and that accidents cannot subsist in God's essence, so they denied that such speech subsists in God. The Hashwiyya, by contrast, ignorantly affirm that temporal accidents subsist in God, which is impossible. Finally, Sanusi points out that all three groups (Jews, Christians, Mu'tazila, and by extension Hashwiyya) are united in their failure to grasp the true doctrine of the People of Truth (Ahl al-Haqq): that God's speech is a non-lettered, non-sounded attribute subsisting in His essence, expressed through various external forms.

Key Terms
الحشوية
اليهود
النصارى
الجسم
حلول الكلام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Hashwiyya are an independent sect with original doctrines.
Correction: Sanusi shows that their creed is actually a composite of existing heresies from Judaism and Christianity, not an original formulation.

Checkpoint
Which group does Sanusi say the Hashwiyya follow in believing God is a body?
A) Christians
B) Jews
C) Mu'tazila
D) Zoroastrians

Key Takeaway
The Hashwiyya's doctrine is not original but a fusion of Jewish anthropomorphism and Christian incarnationism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How does learning that the Hashwiyya adopted corporealism from Judaism challenge you to purify your own understanding of tawhid? What concrete steps will you take today to replace any lingering mental images with true transcendence?
?
The Hashwiyya borrowed beliefs from Christians about divine speech dwelling in creation. Have you ever attributed sacred power to physical objects—like a prayer rug, a Quran stand, or a specific location—as if Allah's speech or presence were literally inside them? Examine your heart: is there any attachment to created things that you need to uproot in repentance?
?
The Hashwiyya combined errors without realizing the contradiction with pure tawhid. What popular beliefs among Muslims today do you suspect might have crept in from other traditions? Are you willing to investigate them with the same hard look Sanusi gave the Hashwiyya, even if it means abandoning something comfortable? Commit to one such belief to verify by the end of this week.

The Hashwiyya's Mu'tazilizing Deviation and the True Doctrine
Sanusi explains the Hashwiyya's third deviation and presents the orthodox view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Hashwiyya's agreement with the Mu'tazila on the nature of divine speech and their crucial difference regarding its subsistence in God.

Core Idea
The Hashwiyya and Mu'tazila agree on letters-and-sounds speech, but differ on whether it subsists in God.

After aligning with Jews and Christians, the Hashwiyya also borrow from the Mu'tazila. They agree that divine speech consists of letters and sounds, but unlike the Mu'tazila, they ignorantly affirm that these temporal accidents subsist in God. Sanusi then presents the true doctrine of the People of Truth.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage noting the agreement with Mu'tazila
Identify the common ground: both Hashwiyya and Mu'tazila believe divine speech is letters and sounds.
The phrase 'ومع المعتزلة في اعتقاد أن كلام الله تعالى حروف وأصوات' is key.
2
Identify the crucial difference
Notice that the Mu'tazila denied that letters and sounds subsist in God, while the Hashwiyya affirm it out of ignorance.
The Mu'tazila reasoned that temporal accidents cannot subsist in God.
3
Learn the orthodox position
Sanusi concludes by stating the true doctrine: divine speech is a non-lettered, non-sounded attribute subsisting in God's essence.
This is the speech of the People of Truth (Ahl al-Haqq).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

However, Sanusi highlights a key difference: the Mu'tazila, due to their rational acumen, realized that letters and sounds are temporal accidents and that accidents cannot subsist in God's essence, so they denied that such speech subsists in God. The Hashwiyya, by contrast, ignorantly affirm that temporal accidents subsist in God, which is impossible. Finally, Sanusi points out that all three groups (Jews, Christians, Mu'tazila, and by extension Hashwiyya) are united in their failure to grasp the true doctrine of the People of Truth (Ahl al-Haqq): that God's speech is a non-lettered, non-sounded attribute subsisting in His essence, expressed through various external forms.

Key Terms
المعتزلة
كلام نفسي
الحوادث
الضروريات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila and Hashwiyya hold identical views on divine speech.
Correction: While both say speech is letters and sounds, the Mu'tazila deny that such speech subsists in God, whereas the Hashwiyya affirm it, leading to a critical theological error.

Checkpoint
Why did the Mu'tazila deny that letters and sounds subsist in God?
A) They believed letters and sounds are eternal
B) They recognized letters and sounds as temporal accidents
C) They followed Jewish doctrine
D) They were ignorant of necessary truths

Key Takeaway
The Hashwiyya's error lies in affirming that temporal letters and sounds subsist in God, whereas the orthodox view is that divine speech is an eternal, non-material attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Sanusi describes the Hashwiyya as being 'greatly stupid' for not using their intellect to avoid attributing temporality to Allah. Is there any belief about Allah that you hold simply because you heard it from others, without reflecting on whether it truly befits His majesty? What will you do to investigate it today?
?
The Mu'tazila used their intellect to avoid a wrong belief, yet they fell into another error. Do you trust that your own reasoning about Allah's attributes is always correct, or do you humbly submit to the guidance of the scholars of Ahl al-Sunna? What is one attribute of Allah you need to re-learn from qualified teachers?
?
The passage says all three groups failed to grasp a 'speech that is not letters or sounds'—something beyond human experience. Have you ever felt uncomfortable with the idea of Allah's speech being non-physical, and do you tend to imagine it as something familiar? How can you train your heart to accept Allah's transcendence even when it defies your imagination?

Refutation of the Hashwiyya: God Never Ceases to Speak
Sanusi refutes the Hashwiyya doctrine of divine silence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi rejects the possibility of God being silent and how he proves the eternity of God's speech.

Core Idea
God has never ceased and will never cease to speak.

Sanusi now turns to refute a deviant sect that claimed God could become silent. He states their doctrine and then offers a logical proof showing that silence would imply createdness of speech. This passage reaffirms the unceasing nature of divine speech.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the structure: statement of false doctrine, affirmation, proof, dismissal.
Pay attention to the logical connection 'idh law jāza...'
2
Identify the false doctrine
Find the phrase where Sanusi describes the Hashwiyya belief. What do they attribute to God?
The key word is 'sukūt' (silence).
3
Follow the logical proof
Trace the argument: if silence is possible, then speech could cease to exist, which would make it created (hādith). Why is that a problem?
God's speech is traditionally held to be eternal.
4
Understand the dismissal
Sanusi rejects the Hashwiyya's excuse of 'kumūn' (concealment) as groundless. What is the force of his dismissal?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نفي ما ذهبَتْ إليه الحشوية مِنْ نسبة السكوت له تعالى، قوله: (أو يطرأ عليه سكوت) إشارة إلى مذهب الحشويَّةِ الذينَ وصفوا كلامَهُ تعالى بالسكوتِ، تعالى الله عن قولهم علوّاً كبيراً، بل لم يزل سبحانَهُ مُتكلّماً ولا يزالُ؛ إذ لو جازَ أنْ يسكت جلَّ وعلا عن كلامِهِ لجازَ أنْ يتّصفَ كلامُهُ بالعدمِ، وذلك يُوجِبُ حدوثه، وما ادعاه الحشويَّةُ مِنْ كُمُونِ الكلامِ معَ السكوتِ.. هوس لا حاصل له
Refutation of what the Hashwiyya have gone to of attributing silence to Him, exalted is He. His statement: 'Or silence could occur to Him' is an indication of the doctrine of the Hashwiyya who described His speech, exalted is He, as silence. Exalted is God above their saying with a great exaltation. Rather, He has never ceased to be speaking and will not cease to be; for if it were permissible for Him, exalted and high, to be silent concerning His speech, it would be permissible for His speech to be characterized by non-existence, and that necessitates its temporality. And what the Hashwiyya claim of the concealment of speech with silence is a delirious fancy with no substance.
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal objection-and-answer (refutation). Sanusi begins by stating the topic: refuting the Hashwiyya who attribute silence to God. He references a phrase 'or silence could occur to Him' likely from an opponent. He then affirms that God has never ceased and will never cease to be speaking (lam yazal mutakalliman wa la yazalu). The logical proof is: if silence were possible, then God's speech could cease to exist, which would mean it is created (hadith), contradicting its eternity. The Hashwiyya's claim that speech can be concealed (kumun) during silence is dismissed as groundless nonsense (hawas la hasila lahu).

Correction: God never ceases to speak; silence would imply createdness of speech, which is impossible for the eternal attribute.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi proves that divine speech is eternal by showing that silence would entail its creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The Hashwiyya tried to limit God by reducing Him to human categories like silence. Where in your life might you be limiting God by projecting your own limitations onto Him? Confess one area where you have acted as if God's power or attributes were finite, and commit to reminding yourself of His eternal perfection today.

God's Speech to Moses: Eternal, Not Temporal
Sanusi explains the correct interpretation of God's speech to Moses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's speech to Moses was not a temporal event but the perception of His eternal speech, and how this refutes the Mu'tazilite view.

Core Idea
God's speech is eternal; Moses perceived it, not a new creation.

The Mu'tazila argued that God's speech to Moses implies change, as if God began speaking after silence. Sunni scholars, however, maintain that God's speech is an eternal attribute. In this passage, Sanusi clarifies the true meaning of the verse 'Allah spoke to Moses directly' and explains why the term 'silence' is used metaphorically.

Guided Path
1
Context: Refuting the Mu'tazila
Recognize that this passage is a preface for debating the Mu'tazila, who believed that God's speech is created and temporal. Understanding this helps you see why Sanusi emphasizes the eternal nature of speech.
2
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how Sanusi starts with a metaphorical use of 'silence' and then explains the correct interpretation.
Pay attention to the phrase 'alladhi huwa 'adam idrāk' – it is key to the metaphor.
3
Identify the metaphorical use of 'silence'
Sanusi says that when the hadith speaks of God's 'silence', it means figuratively that humans do not perceive His speech. This does not mean God actually falls silent.
The metaphor is based on the human experience of not hearing from a silent person.
4
Understand the actual meaning
The verse does not mean God began speaking after silence, nor that He fell silent after speaking. Rather, God removed a barrier from Moses so he could perceive His eternal speech, then restored the barrier.
This preserves the attribute of eternal speech without change in God.
5
Apply to Paradise
The same principle applies to the people of Paradise: they will perceive God's eternal speech when He removes the barrier. This shows the consistency of the doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلتُ : [ توطئة للكلام مع المعتزلة في قوله تعالى : وَكَلَّمَ اللَّهُ مُوسَى تَكْلِيمًا ] يعني أنَّهُ تجوَّزَ بإطلاقِ الصمتِ على لازمه ؛ وهو عدم إدراك ما عند الصامتِ مِنَ الخبر ، وبهذا تعرفُ أنَّه ليس معنى ) كلَّمَ الله موسى تكليماً ( أنَّهُ ابتدأ الكلامَ لهُ بعدَ أَنْ كانَ ساكتاً ، ولا أَنَّهُ بعد ما كلَّمَهُ انقطعَ كلامه وسكت ، تعالى عن ذلك علوّاً كبيراً ، وإنَّما المعنى : أَنَّهُ تعالى بفضلِهِ أزالَ المانع عن موسى عليه السلام وخلق لهُ سمعاً وقواه حتى أدركَ به كلامَهُ القديم ، ثم منعَهُ بعد وردَّهُ إلى ما كانَ قبل سماع كلامِهِ ، وهكذا معنى كلامه لأهل الجنَّةِ
I say: [A preface for speech with the Muʿtazila regarding His statement: 'And Allah spoke to Moses directly'] — meaning that he used 'silence' metaphorically for its necessary concomitant, which is the non-perception of what the silent one has of information. By this you know that the meaning of 'Allah spoke to Moses directly' is not that He began speech for him after having been silent, nor that after He spoke to him His speech ceased and He fell silent — exalted is He above that greatly. Rather the meaning is that He the Exalted, by His favor, removed the barrier from Moses, peace be upon him, and created for him hearing and strength so that he perceived with it His eternal speech, then afterwards prevented him and returned him to what he had been before hearing His speech. And such is the meaning of His speech to the people of Paradise.
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage is a preface for debating the Muʿtazila concerning the verse 'And Allah spoke to Moses directly' (Q 4:164). The author explains that when the hadith uses 'silence' regarding God, it is metaphorical, referring to the human experience of not perceiving information from a silent person. Thus, 'Allah spoke to Moses' does not mean God began speaking after a period of silence, nor that His speech stopped after that. Rather, God eternally speaks; what happened is that He removed the barrier from Moses and granted him the ability to perceive His eternal speech. Afterwards, He withdrew that ability, returning Moses to his normal state. The same understanding applies to God's speech to the people of Paradise.

Key Terms
الكلام القديم
الصمت
المعتزلة
إدراك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's speech to Moses was a temporal event, implying change in God's attributes.
Correction: God's speech is eternal; Moses perceived it through a temporary removal of a barrier, not through God initiating speech.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what does 'Allah spoke to Moses directly' mean?
A) God created new speech for Moses
B) Moses perceived God's eternal speech
C) God changed from silent to speaking
D) Moses heard sounds from a tree

Key Takeaway
God's speech is eternal; Moses perceived it by God's grace through a temporary removal of the barrier, not through any change in God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Quran, do you truly believe you are hearing the eternal speech of God, the same speech that Moses heard? What would change in your heart if you approached every verse with that certainty? Commit to one practical step to make your Quran recitation more reverent this week.
?
Moses had a barrier removed to perceive God's speech. Is there a barrier in your life—distraction, sin, or arrogance—that prevents you from truly hearing God's words? What concrete action can you take today to lower that barrier?
?
If God's speech is eternal and unchanging, then every time you open the Quran, you are encountering the very voice of the Eternal. Does your daily reading reflect that awe? Or do you treat it as just another book? Decide now to read the next page as if God Himself were speaking directly to you.

Refuting the Mu'tazilite Interpretation of Divine Speech
Refutes Mu'tazilite claim by linking it to denial of eternal speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazilite interpretation of God's speech to Moses as created sounds is rejected because it is built on the denial of God's eternal attribute of speech.

Core Idea
Mu'tazilite interpretation of divine speech denies God's eternal attribute.

In this passage, Sanusi addresses a specific Mu'tazilite claim about how God spoke to Moses. The Mu'tazila suggested that God created letters and sounds in a tree which Moses heard. Sanusi points out that this interpretation stems from their denial of God's eternal speech. Since he has already refuted that denial earlier, he dismisses this interpretation as built on a corrupt foundation.

Guided Path

2
Identify the Basis of Refutation

Sanusi's Refutation of the Mu'tazila on Attribution vs. Predicate
Sanusi refutes the Mu'tazila claim that the dispute is about attribution, not the predicate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the Mu'tazila claim that the dispute is about attribution, not the predicate, by exposing the implications of their reinterpretation of 'kallama' as 'created speech'.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila cannot escape being trapped by their own redefinition of 'kallama'.

Sanusi now addresses a second objection from the Mu'tazila: they claim the disagreement is only about the attribution of speech to God, not about the predicate itself. He denies this and shows that their reinterpretation of 'kallama' as 'created speech' is actually metaphorical, not literal. If they insist it is literal, then only Allah would be a true speaker, which contradicts their own principles.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Let Sanusi's argument unfold.
Pay attention to the terms 'nisba' and 'musnad'.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila claim
The Mu'tazila argue that the dispute is about the attribution (nisba) of speech to Allah, not about the predicate (musnad) itself. Understand their starting point.
3
Understand Sanusi's denial and reasoning
Sanusi denies this claim. He shows that the Mu'tazila agree attribution is literal, but they reinterpret speech as created, making 'kallama' metaphorical. The emphatic verbal noun (tawkid) in the verse prevents this metaphorical interpretation.
Notice how Sanusi uses the Mu'tazila's own agreement against them.
4
Examine the alternative scenario
If the Mu'tazila insist that 'kallama' literally means 'created', then the dispute becomes a linguistic one. Worse, it follows that only Allah is a true speaker, since only He creates. This contradicts their own principles.
This exposes a logical consequence of their position.
5
Reflect on the refutation's strength
Consider why Sanusi dismisses the Mu'tazila's denial based on their corrupt principle of originated power. His refutation is airtight because it uses their own premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الثاني : منْعُ أنَّ النزاعَ إنَّما هو في النسبةِ لا المسندِ ، وذلكَ أنَّ المعتزلة موافقون على أنَّ إسناد الكلامِ إِلى اللهِ تعالى حقيقةٌ لا مجاز ، وأنَّهُ هو الذي كلَّمَ موسى لا غيرُه ، لكن تأوّلوا الكلامَ المسند إليهِ على معنى الخلْقِ للكلام ، ومعنى ( كلَّمَ ) عندهم : خلق الكلام ، والمتكلّم عندَهم : الخالق للكلام ، ولا شكَّ أنَّ استعمالَ ( كلَّمَ ( بمعنى ( خلقَ الكلامَ ) مجاز ، فتوكيده بالمصدر يدفعه وإن زعم المعتزلة أنَّ ) كلَّمَ ( بمعنى ( خلق ) هو الحقيقة ، وغيرَه مجاز . . كان النزاعُ معَهم لغوياً ، ويلزمُهم أن لا مُتكلّم حقيقةً إلا الله تعالى ؛ إذ لا خالق سواه ، ومنعهم لذلك بمقتضى أصلهم الفاسد في تأثير القدرةِ الحادثة في مقدورها لا يُسْمَعُ ؛ لفسادِهِ
The answer to the second [objection]: denial that the dispute is only about the attribution not the predicate; because the Mu'tazila agree that the attribution of speech to Allah Most High is literal, not metaphorical, and that He is the one who spoke to Musa, no other, but they interpret the speech attributed to Him as meaning the creation of speech, and the meaning of 'kallama' for them is 'created speech', and the one who speaks (al-mutakallim) for them is the creator of speech. There is no doubt that using 'kallama' in the sense of 'created speech' is metaphorical, so its emphasis with the verbal noun prevents it. And if the Mu'tazila claim that 'kallama' in the sense of 'created' is the literal meaning, and other than it is metaphorical... then the dispute with them is linguistic, and it follows from them that there is no one who speaks literally except Allah Most High, since there is no creator other than Him. Their denial of that due to their corrupt principle of the effect of the originated power on its object is not heeded, because of its corruption.
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage is a response to a second objection. The opponent argued that the dispute with the Mu'tazila is only about the attribution (nisba) of speech to God, not about the predicate (musnad) itself. The author denies this. He explains that the Mu'tazila agree that attributing speech to God is literal, but they reinterpret the speech itself as 'created speech', so they take 'kallama' to mean 'created'. This makes 'kallama' metaphorical in their usage, and the emphatic verbal noun in the verse prevents that metaphorical interpretation. If the Mu'tazila insist that 'kallama' in the sense of 'created' is literal, then the dispute becomes merely linguistic, and a logical consequence follows: only Allah would be a true speaker (mutakallim) because only He creates. The Mu'tazila would deny this consequence based on their corrupt principle of the effect of originated power (qudra ḥāditha) on its object, but that principle is unsound and thus their denial is not accepted.

Key Terms
النسبة
المسند
المتكلم
الخلق
المجاز
التوكيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mu'tazila completely deny that Allah speaks.
Correction: They agree attribution is literal but reinterpret speech as created; they do not deny speech altogether.

Checkpoint
What logical consequence follows if the Mu'tazila claim that 'kallama' meaning 'created' is literal?
A) Only Allah is a true speaker.
B) Humans can create speech like Allah.
C) The emphatic verbal noun becomes meaningless.
D) The dispute is about the predicate, not attribution.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi refutes the Mu'tazila by showing that their reinterpretation of 'kallama' as 'created' either makes it metaphorical (contradicting their claim) or leads to the unacceptable consequence that only Allah is a true speaker.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
Which of Allah's attributes are you tempted to reinterpret because accepting it entirely would challenge your current worldview? How would your life change if you took it literally?

Defending the Imams from Weak Reports: Dialectical Context
Sanusi defends imams from weak reports and explains dialectical context.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how statements made by great imams in dialectical debates are not necessarily their settled doctrines, and the principle of excluding such statements from their established schools.

Core Idea
Not every scholar's statement is his doctrine; context matters.

This passage addresses a common problem in Islamic theological history: reports of controversial statements attributed to major imams. Sanusi clarifies that such statements may have been uttered in the heat of dialectical debate to gradually convince an opponent, not as the imam's actual belief. He invokes the principle that debate statements should not be taken as formal doctrine.

Guided Path
1
Understand the problem: false attribution
Read the opening sentence where Sanusi states his certainty about the exoneration of the imams from the weak reports. Notice the strong language: 'without hesitation'.
2
Learn the dialectical context explanation
Sanusi suggests that such statements were made in dialectical debates to gradually bring a stubborn opponent to the truth. This is not the imam's actual belief.
The phrase 'لإفحام خصم' (to refute an opponent) indicates the purpose.
3
See the principle from the masters
The masters said: 'What is issued from a scholar on the way of inquiry should not be transmitted and made his doctrine.' This is the key methodological rule.
This principle prevents taking debate statements as authoritative.
4
Examine al-Sharif's example and application
Al-Sharif explains that the statements of al-Qadi and al-Ustadh about the originated power were only for debate, and they actually affirmed consensus on attributing creation to Allah alone. Sanusi then applies this reasoning to the more severe reports from Imam al-Haramayn.
This shows how the principle works in practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ تنزيه الأئمَّةِ المحققين عن مثل هذه الأقوال الضعيفة الهالكة ] وبالجملة : فالذي أقطعُ بِهِ مِنْ غيرِ تردُّدٍ : تنزه هؤلاء الأئمة عمَّا نُقِلَ عنهم ، ولعلَّ ذلكَ إنَّما صدر عنهم في مناظرة جدلية لإفحام خصمِ قويَتْ منافرته للحقِّ ، فاحتالوا لسوقه إلى الحقِّ بتدريج ، ولهذا قال المشايخ : لا يُنقَلُ عنِ العالم ويُجعَلَ مذهباً له ما يصدر عنه على سبيل البحث «وقد قالَ الشريف في شرح الأسرار العقلية » نحو هذا ؛ قال : ( ما يُنسَبُ للقاضي والأستاذ - يعني : مِنْ كون القدرة الحادثة تُؤثر في الحالِ - إِنَّما صدرَ منهما ذلك على وجه المناظرة للخصوم ، وإلا فحاشى القاضي والأستاذ أنْ يعتقدا أثراً لغير القدرة القديمة ، كيف وقد نقل الإجماع في مواضعَ مِنْ كُتُبِهِ على كفْرِ مَنْ نسبَ الاختراعَ لغير الله تعالى(۱)، ونقل أيضاً إجماع الأمَّةِ على كفرِ مَنْ لم يقل بعموم صفات الباري تعالى ؟! ((۲) قلتُ : وإذا قال هذا في مقالة القاضي والأستاذ معَ خفَّتِها بالنسبة إلى ما نقل عن إمام الحرمين .. فكيف بتلك المقالة الشنيعة التي نُقِلَتْ عن الإمام ممَّا لا يرضى أنْ يقولَها مَنْ هو أدنى منه علماً وديناً بمراتب كثيرة ؟! ولقد ابتلينا بأقوال باطلةٍ تُنسَبُ لأئمَّةِ السنَّةِ ، والله تعالى يعلم هل صدرَتْ منهم أم لا ، وعلى تقدير صدورها فعلى أي وجه صدرَتْ ، وهو سبحانه حسيبُ مَنْ ينقلُ مثل هذهِ الأقوال الفاسدة على وجهِ يتراخي في بيان فسادِها أو دفعِها عمَّنْ لا تليقُ بهِ إِنْ أمكنه ذلكَ ، وبالله التوفيق .
And in summary: what I am certain of without hesitation is the exoneration of these imams from what has been narrated about them, and perhaps that was only issued from them in a dialectical debate to refute an opponent whose aversion to truth was strong, so they resorted to bringing him to the truth gradually. For this reason, the masters said: 'What is issued from a scholar on the way of inquiry should not be transmitted and made his doctrine.' And al-Sharif said in Sharh al-Asrar al-'Aqliyya something like this; he said: 'What is attributed to al-Qadi and al-Ustadh — meaning concerning the temporally originated power having an effect in the moment — that was only issued from them in the way of debating opponents. Otherwise, far be it from al-Qadi and al-Ustadh to believe in an effect for other than the eternal power. How could it be, when he has transmitted consensus in places of his books on the disbelief of one who attributes creation to other than Allah, and also transmitted the consensus of the umma on the disbelief of one who does not affirm the universality of the attributes of the Exalted God?! I say: And if he says this regarding the statement of al-Qadi and al-Ustadh, despite its lightness compared to what is transmitted from Imam al-Haramayn, then how about that hideous statement transmitted from the Imam which none who is lower than him in knowledge and religion by many degrees would be content to say?! And indeed we have been afflicted with false statements attributed to the imams of the Sunnah, and Allah knows whether they were issued from them or not, and if they were issued, in what manner they were issued. And He is the sufficient Reckoner of one who transmits such corrupt statements in a way that hesitates to explain their corruption or to defend against them those who are not worthy of them, if that is possible. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 449

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the great imams against false attributions. Sanusi begins by stating his certainty that the imams are exonerated from the weak reports. He suggests a possible context: such statements were made in dialectical debates (munazara jadaliyya) to gradually bring an opponent to the truth, not as settled doctrine. He cites the principle that statements made in debate should not be taken as the scholar's madhhab. Then he quotes al-Sharif al-Jurjani's explanation that the reports from al-Qadi (al-Baqillani) and al-Ustadh (al-Isfara'ini) about the temporally originated power having an effect were in the context of debating opponents, not belief. Al-Sharif adds that they even transmitted consensus on the disbelief of one who attributes creation to other than Allah. Sanusi then applies this reasoning to the more severe reports from Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni). He ends by lamenting the spread of false statements and leaving their reckoning to Allah.

Key Terms
تنزيه
مناظرة جدلية
مذهب
إمام الحرمين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Every statement attributed to a scholar is his settled opinion.
Correction: Sanusi explains that such statements may have been made in dialectical debates to gradually guide an opponent, and the masters ruled that statements made 'on the way of inquiry' should not be transmitted as doctrine.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why might statements attributed to the imams not be their actual doctrine?
A) They were made in dialectical debates to gradually guide an opponent.
B) They were mistakes in transmission.
C) The imams were forced to say them.
D) They are all fabrications.

Key Takeaway
Statements made in dialectical debate should not be taken as the settled doctrine of the scholar.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a controversial statement attributed to a scholar, do you immediately accept it or reject it without considering the context? This lesson calls you to pause and investigate. What one step will you take this week to verify before sharing such reports?
?
Think of a time you judged a scholar or a group based on an isolated quote. Does this passage challenge you to reconsider that judgment? Are you willing to seek the full context and possibly change your view?
?
Sanusi laments that false statements afflict the community. Have you ever contributed to spreading such statements, even unintentionally? Will you commit to being a source of accurate knowledge rather than a channel for misinformation? How will you hold yourself accountable?
